From MW_Plug at Num9.com  Mon Nov  1 12:22:21 2004
From: MW_Plug at Num9.com (Mike Witt)
Date: Mon, 1 Nov 2004 12:22:21 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Too much swap space?
Message-ID: <40580.192.168.0.254.1099340541.squirrel@192.168.0.254>

It it possible to have too much swap space? For example, I've
got 256 Meg of Ram on a Fedora Core 2 box, and a full 1 Gig of
swap space configured. Is there any way I could be suffering
a performance *penalty* for having too much swap? (I've got
plenty of disk space, so I'm not worried about "wasting" a
little space.)

-Mike



From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Mon Nov  1 12:55:59 2004
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Mon, 01 Nov 2004 12:55:59 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Too much swap space?
In-Reply-To: <40580.192.168.0.254.1099340541.squirrel@192.168.0.254>
References: <40580.192.168.0.254.1099340541.squirrel@192.168.0.254>
Message-ID: <1099342559.27442.12.camel@denk.nakedape.priv>

On Mon, 2004-11-01 at 12:22 -0800, Mike Witt wrote:
> It it possible to have too much swap space? For example, I've
> got 256 Meg of Ram on a Fedora Core 2 box, and a full 1 Gig of
> swap space configured. Is there any way I could be suffering
> a performance *penalty* for having too much swap? (I've got
> plenty of disk space, so I'm not worried about "wasting" a
> little space.)

No; if you find the kernel swapping more than it should (instead of
reclaiming cache pages), there is tuning you can do via sysctl.

Wil

From buchholz at easystreet.com  Mon Nov  1 15:53:13 2004
From: buchholz at easystreet.com (Don Buchholz)
Date: Mon, 01 Nov 2004 15:53:13 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] GIMP-2: "Thinning" an Image
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.60.0410281756110.10521@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.60.0410281756110.10521@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <4186CC69.1000109@easystreet.com>

Rich Shepard wrote:

>   I cannot find a menu option that would let me "thin" a 1.1M image so 
> that
> it will load faster as part of my Web pages. Does anyone know the secret?

Saving to PNG and JPG formats allow you to specify a compression
factor.  You can also toggle an "interlaced" setting.  IIRC, the interlacing
effect feeds the browser a coarse image (every other line?) which it can
begin to paint, and then as the rest of the data is retrieved the image
begins to sharpen up.

- Don

From holger001 at frogleg.net  Mon Nov  1 18:47:36 2004
From: holger001 at frogleg.net (Holger Stephan)
Date: Mon, 01 Nov 2004 18:47:36 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] GIMP-2: "Thinning" an Image
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.60.0411011626560.18189@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.60.0410281756110.10521@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<4186CC69.1000109@easystreet.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.60.0411011626560.18189@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <1099363656.6982.411.camel@halo.glassword.com>

On Mon, 2004-11-01 at 16:29, Rich Shepard wrote:
> Don,
> 
>    Thanks to all of you.
> 
>    I discovered that while the GIMP reports "1.2M background" the image file
> size is only 23K. What the higher value represents I've no idea, but the
> image does load quickly so it's a non-issue.
> 

Rich: Gimp tells you how much memory the image consumes in uncompressed
format. That's just the number of pixel times color depth. 

Someone asked about the JPEG quality setting. For web images a setting
between 0.6~0.8 is good in most cases. I found a 800x600 pixel JPG image
with good size/quality compromise very often ends up around 80 kByte,
regardless of quality settings. 

Interlaced images are downloaded and rendered in several passes,
skipping lines until it is fully loaded. It is seen earlier in the
download process but the resulting image is also a bit larger. 

- Holger

From aaron at bavariati.org  Mon Nov  1 20:24:49 2004
From: aaron at bavariati.org (Aaron Burt)
Date: Mon, 1 Nov 2004 20:24:49 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Debian's Perl module Math::Matrix package?
In-Reply-To: <86vfcp9dfh.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
References: <86vfcp9dfh.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
Message-ID: <20041102042449.GD10348@bavariati.org>

On Mon, Nov 01, 2004 at 06:45:22PM -0800, Russell Senior wrote:
> 
> I am looking at some Perl code that uses the Math::Matrix module.
> Does anyone happen to know which Debian package that might reside in? 

By typical Debian naming, it'd be in libmath-matrix-perl.  But it
doesn't show up in apt-cache, so you'll have to get it from CPAN.
Nicest way I've found is to use dh-make-perl, which can pull the
module from CPAN and make it into a Debian package.

As most of you know, Microsharp is no longer hosting pdxLinux.org.
Our good friends at Naked Ape Consulting <www.nakedape.com> are taking 
this over, and there may be some outages during the transition period.
At the present time:

       -  www.pdxLinux.org is down.
       -  The major PLUG mailing lists (plug at pdxLinux.org, 
          PLUG-announce at pdxLinux.org, PLUG-devel at pdxLinux.org, 
          PLUG-jobs at pdxLinux.org, PLUG-talk at pdxLinux.org, 
          PLUG-test at pdxLinux.org, and PLUG-web at pdxLinux.org) have
          been moved to the new site and should be completely
          operational.
       -  Archives for these lists are available at either:
                   http://mail.pdxLinux.org
                or
                   http://lists.pdxLinux.org


We hope to have the main web site up before long.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Mon Nov  1 21:20:17 2004
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Mon, 1 Nov 2004 21:20:17 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] News about the dirvish disk-to-disk backup program
Message-ID: <20041102052017.GA31032@gate.kl-ic.com>


Back in March 2004, I gave a presentation at the PLUG meeting about dirvish,
an rsync-based disk-to-disk backup program.  There have been quite a 
bit of news since then - if you don't care, hit "n".

There is a website and a wiki and a mailing list:
   http://www.dirvish.org/
   http://www.dirvish.org/wiki
   http://www.dirvish.org/mailman/listinfo/dirvish

Stop by and look.

The most important news is that the author of dirvish, Jonathan W.
Schultz, drowned in Lake Mead in March.  I found out about it in late May,
and I set up the sites above to maintain and develop the software.  More
needs to be done, and experienced perl hackers are welcome to contribute.

Other news is that I just set up a mailing list with Mailman and Postfix
and a virtual domain, and the experience was somewhat nasty (and perhaps
incorrectly done).  If anyone has experience with these, I could probably
use some list testing help.  The wiki was a lot easier to set up.

Finally, I am working with Hal Pomerantz, technical editor of Sys Admin
magazine, on a three part article for the magazine.  Hopefully the first
part will be in the January issue.  Anyone who wants to contribute to
testing the examples, or wants to contribute some embarassing questions
that articles 2 (setup) and 3 (restore) should answer, let me know.

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

From mikeraz at patch.com  Tue Nov  2 06:17:19 2004
From: mikeraz at patch.com (Michael Rasmussen)
Date: Tue, 2 Nov 2004 06:17:19 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] (OT) My Father's Vision (washingtonpost.com)
Message-ID: <20041102141719.GB15162@patch.com>


http://www.washingtonpost.com/wp-dyn/articles/A17264-2004Nov1.html

It's not right to fully quote an article.  This one is too good to
allow for the chance that you might not click through.

 My Father's Vision

 By Gene Weingarten
 Tuesday, November 2, 2004; Page A21

 My father, Philip Weingarten, is 90. He wears hearing aids in both
 ears. At night, before he goes to bed, he removes them as he stands with
 his head above an open dresser drawer. That is because, if he dropped
 one on the floor, he could never find it. He cannot see the floor.

 His right eye sees nothing. The sight that remains in his left eye is so
 limited that, to read, he must use a machine that magnifies each letter
 to the size of a baseball. In this manner, he reads The Washington Post
 every day, all day. The retina in his functioning eye is a rubble of
 scar tissue, so ravaged from diabetic damage and macular degeneration
 that his doctor doesn't know how he sees anything at all. Somehow his
 brain has learned to cobble together into vaguely recognizable images
 the fractured signals from the few neurons that still fire.

 When he walks the halls of the assisted-living facility in which
 he lives, people greet him by name, but he cannot do the same in
 return. The friendly blobs are indistinguishable. I have always joked
 that my father is the reincarnation of Archduke Franz Ferdinand, heir
 to the Austro-Hungarian throne. That is because he was born on June 28,
 1914, the day the archduke and his pregnant wife were assassinated,
 touching off World War I. Ferdinand's dying words were selfless --
 concern for his wife and family -- which pretty much sums up the way
 my father has lived his life.

 Though his eyes have betrayed him, his mind remains as sharp as
 yours or mine. He was an accountant, and has attended to his own
 affairs ably until just last week, when he found he could no longer
 see well enough to write checks or keep records. And so my wife and I
 brought him to the bank, where he conferred power of attorney on his
 daughter-in-law. Intending no disrespect, the banker began by directing
 questions to my wife and me, but quickly checked herself when my father
 volunteered not only his Social Security number but the precise number
 of his savings account.

 He moves in a determined shuffle, behind an aluminum walker. When he
 ventures outside, he needs a companion to warn him of the presence
 of a curb, or a sidewalk anomaly. The ground is a soupy mist to him,
 swallowing his legs at the thigh. Daylight can play mischief with
 his eyesight. Sometimes, the empty streets before him are aswarm with
 pint-sized people, or blocked with poplar trees, or are a landscape
 of yawning craters. These are the only things he sees in sharp focus,
 because they bypass his ruined eyes entirely. They are inventions of
 his brain, a medical syndrome not uncommon in people with profound loss
 of vision. They are hallucinations, but not delusions. He knows they
 are unreal; still, it is an act of faith and no small courage to step
 confidently forward into an abyss, just because someone you love tells
 you it is safe.

 It is through this foggy soup and minefield of phantasms that my father
 will walk this afternoon, with me at his side, into a church in suburban
 Maryland. There -- as he has done every four years since he chose
 Roosevelt over Landon -- he will cast a mathematically insignificant
 vote for president of the United States. Because he knows it is the
 right thing to do.

 So, what are you doing today?

On Tue, 2 Nov 2004, Wil Cooley wrote:

> On Tue, 2004-11-02 at 08:39 -0800, Colin Kuskie wrote:
>
>> Use the CPAN shell:
>
> Bzzzt!  Not on my boxes you don't!  It's all happily packaged and 
> installed now.

I'm at the point of just punting when it comes to applications that 
require a gazillion unpackaged Perl modules. RT is a good (or bad, 
depending on your mood) example.

I built a local copy of perl in /usr/local/perl-<vers>/, use CPAN.pm 
to manage modules, and adjust the #! line accordingly in those complex 
applications.

The system perl stays pristine, and the local perl is easy to manage 
(no rpm building necessary).

In some ways it's a headache, but it makes RT management soooo much 
simpler.

I suspect I'd do the same thing if I had to maintain a heavily 
customized python environment...

--Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com>

On Tue, 2 Nov 2004, Wil Cooley wrote:


> Hm, that's in interesting take on it.  It doesn't really deal with 
> the reproducability aspect (well, I guess you can just copy the 
> directory to a new computer).

There are two ways to deal with reproducability (is that really a 
word? :-):

1. Script the build (ala rpm) and then, using the newly built perl
    binary, do all the CPAN installations.

2. Build it by hand, then tar up the directory tree.

Either way make backup/restore/propogate relatively painless. If 
you're so inclined, you can even produce a nosrc rpm and push it 
around the network that way...

--Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com>

Subject: [PLUG] FTP server and Samba mounted shares
Message-ID: <9e0a357804110309397ce09b97@mail.gmail.com>

I'm in the process of migrating a Windows FTP server to Linux.  The
FTP user's home directories are on a Windows file server, however, and
I'm running into permissions issues.  I've used smbmount to mount the
Windows file shares, but user's seem to only have the rights of the
Everyone group, which is very restricted.  I have a mount script that
runs the following for each user to mount their FTP home directory:

su - theuser -c "smbmount //server/share /ftp/theuser -o username=domadmin%pass"

vsftpd runs as the user "ftpsecure", so I'm assuming this is the
problem.  Does anyone have any suggestions for how to get vsftpd to
have domain admin permissions?  Or maybe another solution?
Thanks,
~M

> got 256 Meg of Ram on a Fedora Core 2 box, and a full 1 Gig of
> swap space configured. Is there any way I could be suffering
> a performance *penalty* for having too much swap? (I've got
> plenty of disk space, so I'm not worried about "wasting" a

applicable to Fedora Core 2.

I don't think there would be any performance drop from configuring too
much cache.  However, if you actually USE that cache, I bet you see
some problems...

dia seems to be oriented towards conceptual modeling, not geometric

letters.

What do you use? If you use these, how can I make them work for me?

On Thu, 04 Nov 2004 15:41:31 -0800, Evan Heidtmann
<clydefrog at adnap.no-ip.com> wrote:
> I'm writing a paper in Lyx and I need to insert a couple simple
> geometric diagrams. I've tried dia and kseg.
> 
> dia seems to be oriented towards conceptual modeling, not geometric
> drawing. I can make it work, but the circles come out looking like
> polygons when I use SVG output. Also, I can't figure out how to insert
> greek letters into the drawings.
> 
> kseg is definitely for geometry, but it can't export except to bitmap
> formats. It also seems unable to label angles. I haven't tried greek

Search google something like this:

http://www.google.com/search?q=insert+images+into+latex&start=0&start=0&ie=utf-8&oe=utf-8&client=firefox-a&rls=org.mozilla:en-US:official

and you will find lot's of advice on converting different formats into
eps and sizing and all that.  I believe that will help you out.

<advice type="unsolicited">
Also, if you have gone to the trouble to figure out lyx (assuming
you've transitioned from the world of Word like I did), you really
should read this:

http://www.ctan.org/tex-archive/info/lshort/english/lshort.pdf

and just use LaTeX.  The basic markup you need to get a great looking
print or pdf is amazingly simple, and the amount of help on the web is
even more astounding.  It's quite a liberating experience.
</advice>

Bryan


From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Thu Nov  4 15:57:58 2004
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Thu, 4 Nov 2004 15:57:58 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Geometric Diagrams in Lyx?
In-Reply-To: <418ABE2B.2070107@adnap.no-ip.com>
References: <418ABE2B.2070107@adnap.no-ip.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.60.0411041551570.21066@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Thu, 4 Nov 2004, Evan Heidtmann wrote:

> I'm writing a paper in Lyx and I need to insert a couple simple geometric
> diagrams. I've tried dia and kseg.

   Good for you, Evan!

> What do you use? If you use these, how can I make them work for me?

   I looked at 7 different vector-graphics packages (including xfig which I
found had a steep learning curve for drawing the "bell" curves I need in my
book. So I ended up with "tgif" <http://bourbon.usc.edu:8001/tgif/>.

   I enter Greek and mathematical symbols directly and set the "print"
function to .eps. The figures go right into LyX with no problems. BTW, use
figure floats to insert your images and let LaTeX decide where they are best
positioned.

   Holler if you need help. Bill added my explanation of how to annotate
figures on his FAQ page. The _only_ non-easily-grokked part of the program
is learning that when you hover the cursor over an icon the explanation
shows up on the left side of the status bar at the bottom of the window, not
in a little box by the cursor.

   I have at least a couple of dozen figures in my book, all done with tgif,
and I really like it. They print beautifully using pdxlates.

HTH,

Rich

On Thu, 4 Nov 2004, Bryan Murdock wrote:

> I've certainly never written a book, nor used nested tables like that. I'm
> sure a GUI frontend to LaTeX can make the hairier markup much easier.

Bryan,

   Not only hairy markup, but all of it.

> I'll admit too that I'm a plain text kind of guy.

   I, too, prefer the command line and text editors. That's why I use pine,
slrn, emacs and joe. However, sometimes the productivity increase of the GUI
front end (LyX) or application (Eclipse) makes too much sense to ignore.
That's also why I run the X Window System (using Xfce) and keep 10 virtual
desktops open with multiple virtual terminals and applications.

> Is it possible to write most of the document in LaTeX and then import it
> into LyX, create a hairy table, and the re-export to LaTeX?  Or does that
> totally trash your previous markup?  (I abandonded LyX after writing only
> one paper for school, I never became that familiar with it)

   The best answer I can give you is "maybe". I'm not sure of the status of
relyx, the tool designed to import LaTeX into LyX.

   Years ago I tried learning LaTeX and gave up because the curve was too
steep for the time I had available. Then I discovered LyX at a very early
version. It took me about 15 minutes to go through the tutorial and I
started writing articles and reports with it. As I needed to learn more I
used the two built-in documents under the Help menu and the exceptionally
helpful mail list. Never looked back.

   I've no idea how easy it is to generate the ToC or Lists of Figures/Tables
or cross-references in straight LaTeX. It's a breeze in LyX. And, I don't
have to learn all the proper forms of the markup, the software does that for
me when I press the magic keys or select a menu item.

> Of course, we should all follow the path that Norm Walsh took and start
> using XML to markup our documents, right?

   Nope. What I like is that I will send the .tex output with all the .eps
files (51 so far) to Springer-Verlag and the book will move through
production that much faster: camera-ready copy.

   If folks are interested I can give a tour of LyX at a PLUG meeting. I now
use it for all my writing other than letters and the occasional permit
application. I also find it much easier to read without the distraction of
markup in the words.

Rich

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Thu Nov  4 19:00:15 2004
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Thu, 4 Nov 2004 19:00:15 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Geometric Diagrams in Lyx?
In-Reply-To: <1099620229.7126.8.camel@timmy>

On Thu, 4 Nov 2004, Evan Heidtmann wrote:

> I have tgif installed now. How can I make lines snap to circles? I want
> to draw a circle with two radii.

Evan,

   What I do is use the 7th icon down the left margin's tool bar (splined
line), then click on the "line" icon on the top row, third from the right.
That one changes from a straight line to a spline curve to interpolated
spline and back to straight.

   I'd then draw the two arcs using 'snap-to-grid' mode, then edit them by
hand to get what I want. The editing is with snap mode turned off. Then you
can turn snap mode back on and put the two together.

   Does this help or do I not understand what you want to do?

Rich

From rlucas at tercent.com  Fri Nov  5 13:32:47 2004
From: rlucas at tercent.com (Randall Lucas)
Date: Fri, 05 Nov 2004 13:32:47 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] SMP big box vs. many small boxen for database / web server
	performance.
Message-ID: <418BF17F.2050003@tercent.com>

Hi PLUGgers,

This is something of a classic problem, I'm sure, but I've been having 
trouble finding current reasoning on it (a lot of 1999-era debates on 
SMP are in Google).


1. In a nutshell, for an Apache / Perl / MySQL web application with a 
mix of DB reads and writes (assume there's a 1:5:2 http:dbread:dbwrite 
ratio, very roughly), which of the two configurations below would, in 
your opinion, give the best performance / value ratio?

	A. one web server and one database server, each with 1 unit processor 
and 1 unit RAM;

	OR

	B. a single machine with 2 x 1 unit processor and 2 unit RAM?


2. And corollary to 1.:

	- if A is your choice, are there numbers that make B compelling?  In 
other words, what if B contained quad processors, or 4 units RAM?

	- if the scenario is changed so that you are comparing 2 x (1 unit RAM, 
1 unit CPU) + 1 x (2 unit RAM, 2 unit CPU) versus 1 x (4 unit ram, 4 x 1 
unit CPU), does your choice remain the same?


In effect, my question boils down to the efficacy of putting the 
equivalent number of CPU and RAM chips into a single box where, 
presumably, the failure rate will be lower due to fewer points of 
failure.  Also, it seems that even with a fairly slow bus, local socket 
talk to a MySQL server would be much faster than putting it out over the 
network.  Since the DB will be disk-limited, and the Web app probably 
network-limited, it seems like they would play well together in the same 
chassis.

Thoughts?

-- 
Randall Lucas
Tercent, Inc / SuperSurvey Online Surveys
http://www.supersurvey.com


From heinlein at madboa.com  Fri Nov  5 13:49:13 2004
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Fri, 5 Nov 2004 13:49:13 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] SMP big box vs. many small boxen for database / web server
	performance.
In-Reply-To: <418BF17F.2050003@tercent.com>
References: <418BF17F.2050003@tercent.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0411051338330.4957@pecos.madboa.com>

On Fri, 5 Nov 2004, Randall Lucas wrote:

> 1. In a nutshell, for an Apache / Perl / MySQL web application with 
>    a mix of DB reads and writes (assume there's a 1:5:2 
>    http:dbread:dbwrite ratio, very roughly), which of the two 
>    configurations below would, in your opinion, give the best 
>    performance / value ratio?
>
>    A. one web server and one database server, each with 1 unit
>       processor and 1 unit RAM;
>
>    OR
>
>    B. a single machine with 2 x 1 unit processor and 2 unit RAM?

In my opinion, a single box beats two every time: network lag isn't an 
issue, there are fewer hardware component that can fail, and time 
spent on system administration is reduced significantly.

I'd only go with two boxes if a) you're really stressing the system or 
b) you have plans to make the database available to other applications 
on the network.

If it's a production application, I'd try to budget for two boxes, 
using one for development or, worst case, a hot spare.

Have you done any testing of your application under real-life loads to 
get a feel for what stress you'll be putting on the hardware?

The reason I ask is that I have a really hard time envisioning a 
semi-standard LAMP-ish application coming close to stressing even a 
low-end P4 or Athlon unless you're expecting a daily hit count 
numbering in the hundreds of thousands.

-- Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com>

From AthlonRob at axpr.net  Fri Nov  5 14:26:01 2004
From: AthlonRob at axpr.net (AthlonRob)
Date: Fri, 05 Nov 2004 14:26:01 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] SMP big box vs. many small boxen for database /
	web	server performance.
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0411051419120.4957@pecos.madboa.com>
References: <418BF17F.2050003@tercent.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.61.0411051338330.4957@pecos.madboa.com>

Message-ID: <1099693562.19967.23.camel@dell.linux.box>

On Fri, 2004-11-05 at 14:20 -0800, Paul Heinlein wrote:
> On Fri, 5 Nov 2004, AthlonRob wrote:
> 
> > Look at all the issues Berkeley has had with their LAMP-ish setup. 
> > Or CERN.  Or Scripps.
> 
> Sorry, I don't get the references. What am I missing?

Berkeley wrote some software, Boinc (Berkeley Open Infrastructure for
Network Computing), to run DC projects like their own SETI at Home, CERN's
LHC at Home, or Scripps' Predictor at Home.  It's fairly LAMP-ish software,
although Berkeley is currently using SAMP (Solaris)... it's Apache,
MySQL, and PHP/Perl/Python, with a bit of C and C++ on the client side
(and a bit on the server side).

Those projects have been continually plagued by load issues; they aren't
scaling well at all.  LHC at Home has a decent amount of iron to throw at
it, and with 6000 users can't keep up. Scripps is in the process of
getting a real server set up at the moment.

Google around, check out their websites... they're having issues.  The
OP may find some insight, as the projects have all been switching
between multiple systems and trying a single big system to see how
things work out.

That said, it's a good project... it just needs some work.  :-)

-- 
Rob                                |  If not safe,
   Jabber: athlonrob at axpr.net   |    one can never be free.

> C++ on the client side (and a bit on the server side).

   My question is NOT intended as flame-bait, but to address a DB issue that
used to be a hot topic on mail lists: MySQL vs. PostgreSQL when there is a
high level of writing to the tables.

   My understanding of the issue is that MySQL was optimized for reads so it
was frequently the DB of choice for Web apps where reads outdid writes by
many orders of magnitude. But, postgres was the application of choice when
records were frequently updated.

   Just out of curiosity, has this changed? I don't really care one way or
the other because I stay with postgres for all my applications and I really
don't care what anyone else uses. As I wrote, I'm just curious if MySQL is
now more balanced than I understood it once was.

Thanks,

Rich

From amunk at pdx.edu  Fri Nov  5 18:49:33 2004

Date: Fri, 5 Nov 2004 18:49:33 -0800 (PST)

In-Reply-To: <20041105053312.GA15418@subtend.net>
Message-ID: <Pine.GSO.4.44.0411051837230.11487-100000@gere.odin.pdx.edu>

On Thu, 4 Nov 2004, Kris wrote:

> Any gdb magic I can run to see what exactly is going on?  The
> gdb attempt I tried displas a lot of "no debugging symbols" so
> I think I have a not-friendly-to-debugging version (makes sense
> considering it isn't a cvs release or nightly build).

The debugging symbols are not present if the program was not compiled with
the "-g" switch for gcc present, or if it was present but the resulting
binary was then run through the "strip" utility. Generally, packaged
programs from distros don't have the symbols present, because (IIUC) they
take up memory while the program is running.

I've never used Kooka but here is a clip from the included Handbook...


The main window in Kooka consists of three frames. At the top, see both
icon and a menu panels. Use the mouse to enlarge or reduce the windows
as needed.

The navigation window consists of two tabs, which allow you to switch
between the Preview and the integrated file browser called the Gallery.
The working folder is displayed in the lower part of the window along
with the folder where the scan will be saved.

Make your scanner-dependent settings, which are dependent on the scanner
you have connected, in the lower left window. Usually, these are
settings for resolution, brightness and contrast, scanning mode (e.g.,
color, gray, or binary), and for gamma values.

Configure your settings here first. Afterwards, initiate the preview
scan with Preview. If the Preview tab in the upper window is selected,
see the results there.

Select the various formats in the preview image itself to define the
final dimensions. The User setting is recommended for this, so you
select the area to scan in the preview display with the mouse.

After this is done, click Scan to scan the selection made in the
preview. After scanning, you will be asked in which format to save the
image whether to make this your standard format (without being prompted
to confirm your selection in the future).

Note

If you checked that you do not want to be asked about the save format,
the memory assistant will no longer appear. To change the format some
time in the future, select Settings->Configure Kooka...->Save
Image->Always show memory assistant


The large window shows how the image appears after a final scan.

> I've never used Kooka but here is a clip from the included Handbook...
<snip>
> Make your scanner-dependent settings, which are dependent on the scanner
> you have connected, in the lower left window. Usually, these are
> settings for resolution, brightness and contrast, scanning mode (e.g.,
> color, gray, or binary), and for gamma values.

I experimented last night and found that gocr doesn't seem to care in which 
format the image is saved. I tried all of them and got the same OCR results 
with each.

I figured out the odd thing about the documentation saying to use "binary" but 
not having binary as an option in the program. Turns out that the program 
uses "Lineart" to mean binary. That's consistant with what the Windows driver 
calls it, and while binary conveys the concept to a programmer, line art 
probably works better for a graphic artist. Now that I know the synonymous 
relationship, I no longer care!

From rlucas at tercent.com  Sat Nov  6 16:03:18 2004
From: rlucas at tercent.com (Randall Lucas)
Date: Sat, 06 Nov 2004 16:03:18 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Re: SMP big box vs. many small boxen for database / web
	server performance.
In-Reply-To: <20041106005926.6522A58F4@ulke.nakedape.cc>
References: <20041106005926.6522A58F4@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <418D6646.2030203@tercent.com>

> In my opinion, a single box beats two every time: network lag isn't an 
> issue, there are fewer hardware component that can fail, and time 
> spent on system administration is reduced significantly.

Paul,

Are there any pitfalls that you would advise folks to look out for when 
relying upon a single machine for both database and Web duties?  Keeping 
data and web logs / files on separate spindles is of course a must.  Is 
bus speed a big concern in this case?  Or will disk and network IO be 
much more rate limiting?

And, to Rich:

MySQL and PostgreSQL are nowadays pretty close to each other in 
performance, realistically speaking, and I hold it comes down to a 
choice of style.  The MySQL vs. PostgreSQL debate is a staple of the 
open source world, and it's actually quite an interesting one, but 
should not be rehashed here (there's tons of well-informed stuff on the 
web).  My recommendation is to work with both and see what fits your 
style.  What fit my style immediately many years back was MySQL all the 
way -- it's much friendlier to a novice's set of assumptions and his 
probable problem domain.  Once I got familiar with databases, however, 
PostgreSQL won me over.  At this point, I would almost always choose 
PostgreSQL over MySQL (legacy apps are another matter, however).

Without impugning MySQL, I would suggest that the novice with a more 
leisurely pace, or with a larger-scale project before him choose 
PostgreSQL, while he who must write code to work *now*, damn the 
torpedoes, will probably find MySQL a quicker route (or SQLite, a newish 
delight in the low-end of the market).

Best,

Randall


From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Sat Nov  6 16:54:15 2004
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Sat, 6 Nov 2004 16:54:15 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Re: SMP big box vs. many small boxen for database / web
	server performance.
In-Reply-To: <418D6646.2030203@tercent.com>
References: <20041106005926.6522A58F4@ulke.nakedape.cc>
	<418D6646.2030203@tercent.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.60.0411061642550.32338@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Sat, 6 Nov 2004, Randall Lucas wrote:

> MySQL and PostgreSQL are nowadays pretty close to each other in
> performance, realistically speaking, and I hold it comes down to a choice
> of style.

Randall,

   Impugn away. My feelings won't be hurt. :-)

> Without impugning MySQL, I would suggest that the novice with a more
> leisurely pace, or with a larger-scale project before him choose
> PostgreSQL, while he who must write code to work *now*, damn the
> torpedoes, will probably find MySQL a quicker route (or SQLite, a newish
> delight in the low-end of the market).

   I've been building database applications since dBASE II on Kaypros running
CP/M. What I have seen over and over is the lack of understanding of how to
design and build a relational database, regardless of what DBMS one runs.
About 13 years ago I worked briefly doing some database work for a medical
research project. They were using R:base and had no idea how to normalize
data or run a relational database. So, it was all a humongeous flat file
(with more than 100 columns, IIRC).

   Anytime a patient had an additional bypass or other procedure a new column
was added. All tuples were empty but that one. Just for giggles, I redid the
entire database in Paradox/DOS and discovered several patient records were
duplicated (or triplicated). Different data in each copy, too. When I
pointed that out and mentioned that it just might screw up the statistics
drawn from the data we decided that I should not rock their boat any longer.

   So, I recommend that a novice get two books: one on database design and
normalization and the other a Joe Celko book on SQL. Only after reading both
should that novice be allowed to touch a dbms.

   The analogy I've used for years when folks tell me that they have a GIS
package installed so they can do spatial analyses is that teaching someone
how to use a word processor does not make her/him a writer. The difference
between knowing how to run a computer application and knowing when to run it
and how to set it up to produce useful answers is one of my pet peeves.

   And I do clean the peeves cage regularly and keep it well supplied with
fresh water and dolts for food.

Rich

From aaron at bavariati.org  Sat Nov  6 21:36:05 2004
From: aaron at bavariati.org (Aaron Burt)
Date: Sat, 6 Nov 2004 21:36:05 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] SMP big box vs. many small boxen for database / web server
	performance.
In-Reply-To: <418BF17F.2050003@tercent.com>
References: <418BF17F.2050003@tercent.com>
Message-ID: <20041107053605.GU10348@bavariati.org>

On Fri, Nov 05, 2004 at 01:32:47PM -0800, Randall Lucas wrote:
> 1. In a nutshell, for an Apache / Perl / MySQL web application with a 
> mix of DB reads and writes (assume there's a 1:5:2 http:dbread:dbwrite 
> ratio, very roughly), which of the two configurations below would, in 
> your opinion, give the best performance / value ratio?
> 	A. one web server and one database server, each with 1 unit 
> 	processor and 1 unit RAM;
> 	OR
> 	B. a single machine with 2 x 1 unit processor and 2 unit RAM?

B, since your DB traffic is higher than your net traffic, not that
it'll matter with real world bandwidth.  Also, a single box is easier
to admin and offers fewer points of failure.  *nix handles multiple
things on a single box far more gracefully than the competition.

Honestly, I doubt you'll need the dual proc, even.  Modern CPUs are
really, really fast.  Get it working and tested on a generic
single-proc 3+ GHz box with a good mobo, half gig of RAM and an IDE
drive, and then upgrade from there.  First suggestion would be
mirrored hotswap SCSI disks.

If you simply *must* get a box to stick in the colo right away, get a
2U rackmount from Dell (PE2650's good) or Supermicro.

> 2. And corollary to 1.:
> 	- if A is your choice, are there numbers that make B
> 	compelling?  In other words, what if B contained quad
> 	processors, or 4 units RAM?

If the web frontend was simple and lightweight, and the DB was huge
and handling complex queries.  In that case, you're basically spending
the money on a heavier DB server instead of a separate web server.

> 	- if the scenario is changed so that you are comparing 2 x (1
> 	unit RAM, 1 unit CPU) + 1 x (2 unit RAM, 2 unit CPU) versus 1
> 	x (4 unit ram, 4 x 1 unit CPU), does your choice remain the same?

That's too complex.  Don't make us do your homework for you, man.

> Thoughts?

You're overthinking the problem.  Also, I think you may have some
order-of-magnitude misconceptions of the relative bandwidths of system
buses, LANs, typical Internet connections and DB traffic.

Computer hardware gets cheaper, faster and better really quickly.
Buying just what you need now is generally the best strategy.

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Sun Nov  7 00:52:17 2004
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (Darkhorse)
Date: 07 Nov 2004 00:52:17 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] MAX OSX Panther compromised twice...
Message-ID: <1099817537.22543.42.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>

An iBook has been compromised twice now.  My suspicion is that it's a
BSD tcp/ip stack problem.  Rumor has it that ipfw can be bypassed
through an exploit involving the tcp/ip stack.  Whoever did the
compromise, there were no services running on the machine yet they
managed to mount NFS shares remotely and cause strange udp activity
to occur.  This is a very new iBook and it has already gone back
to CompUSA once for a $250 hard drive pull.  Does the iBook have
a compromisable bios?  Can the owner of this iBook clean it up 
or does it have to go back for another $250 hard drive pull?

Googling, it appears there's a weakness in the help system that can
be exploited when you web browse on the Internet.  There's also an
ssh problem, apparently you can be telnetting without even realizing
it.

BSD is supposedly the system to build a firewall with.  What's wrong
with the kernel and what's the fix?  There's one upgrade kernel from
apple, but I don't know that it fixes any tcp/ip stack problem.
There's supposedly a security patch for the help system, I'm
surprised CompUSA didn't take care of this.

Short of being able to figure out what the weakness is, I've suggested
to my friend that he fix my alpha and use it as a router running Linux 
to protect his iBook.  He went from a pc laptop to an iBook thinking
better security.  Any help appreciated.

     --  Michael C. Robinson

   Aha! I wasn't that negligent. I did make a swap partition (/dev/hda8, when

uses a lot of GUI apps I think I will add more RAM. The 600E will take up to
288M (that's the installed 32M plus two additional 128M modules).

From heinlein at madboa.com  Sun Nov  7 14:15:52 2004
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Sun, 7 Nov 2004 14:15:52 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Re: SMP big box vs. many small boxen for database / web
	server performance.
In-Reply-To: <418D6646.2030203@tercent.com>
References: <20041106005926.6522A58F4@ulke.nakedape.cc>
	<418D6646.2030203@tercent.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.60.0411071409270.8012@localhost.localdomain>

On Sat, 6 Nov 2004, Randall Lucas wrote:

> Are there any pitfalls that you would advise folks to look out for 
> when relying upon a single machine for both database and Web duties? 
> Keeping data and web logs / files on separate spindles is of course 
> a must.  Is bus speed a big concern in this case?  Or will disk and 
> network IO be much more rate limiting?

How complex and/or busy will this application be? If you're expecting 
no more than a few thousand page views per day, and each page few 
generates just a dozen or so simple database queries, then buy a fleet 
of cheap P-IIIs so you have hot-spares a-plenty.

If you're expecting 100,000+ page views per day and/or those pages 
rely on some gnarly database queries -- then you might end up 
stressing some hardware.

Honestly, I don't think any of us are qualified to spec a system for 
your web app unless we understand how busy and complex it will be.

-- Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com>


From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Sun Nov  7 14:11:31 2004
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Sun, 7 Nov 2004 14:11:31 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Very Subtle Bug in X.org-6.8.x
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.60.0411071406290.30435@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

   I was bitten twice by this bug. I hope that this time I remember about it.

   Anywho, if any of you are going to upgrade from X.org-6.7.0 to X.org-6.8.0
or -6.8.1 be aware that X ain't gonna' start when you're done. Of course, if
you're booting into a GUI login you'll not see it, so you'll need to boot
'linux single' to fix the problem.

   The report (and /var/log/X.org.0.log) indicate that the core keyboard
cannot be loaded because there is no such module. Of course, there is
/usr/X11R6/lib/modules/input/keyboard_<something>.o, but ...

   In the revised /etc/xorg.conf's "Keyboard" section you'll see that the
device is listed as:
 	device "Keyboard"
which is why the driver won't load. Change the above line to:
 	device "keyboard"
and life is once again good.

   Case counts!

Rich (and no, I didn't log into the X.org bugzilla to report this)

-- 
Dr. Richard B. Shepard, President
Applied Ecosystem Services, Inc. (TM)
<http://www.appl-ecosys.com>   Voice: 503-667-4517   Fax: 503-667-8863

   The reports now run and finish.

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Mon Nov  8 09:56:00 2004

On Mon, 8 Nov 2004, Roderick A. Anderson wrote:

> How do I determine which and how much of the swap(s) are being used?

Rod,

   I'm reaching far beyond my expertise here for the first part of your
question. For the second part, use 'top'. I suspect that the kernel picks
and choses based on a rather sophisticated algorithm which partition is the
most advantageous at any given moment when swap is needed. Regardless,
probably 1G of swap is sufficient and the 128M could return to its previous
job of being /opt.

Hey Rod...

Do you have any of those SMALLER HD's say 1-4GB range?
If so... install one on your computer... and make the entire HD swap!!

down as much when it swaps because it's using a different drive.

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Mon Nov  8 18:56:31 2004
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Mon, 8 Nov 2004 18:56:31 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] httpd down
In-Reply-To: <200411081846.29720.rsteff.l@comcast.net>
References: <200411081846.29720.rsteff.l@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.60.0411081851090.31931@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Mon, 8 Nov 2004, Richard C. Steffens wrote:

> 2. What's the right way to restart httpd?

Dick,

   If there's no process ID to be found then you cannot do 'SIGHUP pid'.
That's the way to restart a process so it reads its config file after
changes.

   Depends on the distribution. There is an 'apachectl' file somewhere on
your system. In Slackware it's /usr/sbin/apachectl. I recall reading
somewhere that this is the approved way to start httpd. Your distribution
may also have what I have here in /etc/rc.d/rc.httpd:

[rshepard at salmo ~]$ less /etc/rc.d/rc.httpd 
#!/bin/sh
#
# /etc/rc.d/rc.httpd
#
# Start/stop/restart the Apache web server.
#
# To make Apache start automatically at boot, make this
# file executable:  chmod 755 /etc/rc.d/rc.httpd
#

case "$1" in
    'start')
       /usr/sbin/apachectl start ;;
    'stop')
       /usr/sbin/apachectl stop ;;
    'restart')
       /usr/sbin/apachectl restart ;;
    *)
       echo "usage $0 start|stop|restart" ;;
esac

   This does the same thing, but from the sysinit setup.

> 3. Any other thought on this problem are also welcomed.

   Dust bunnies? Boredom? :-)

Rich

From rlucas at tercent.com  Tue Nov  9 11:33:54 2004
From: rlucas at tercent.com (Randall Lucas)
Date: Tue, 09 Nov 2004 11:33:54 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] httpd down
Message-ID: <41911BA2.4080606@tercent.com>

Hi Richard,

1. When you went to restart httpd, you just invoked the program as root 
-- it probably didn't get the right options for startup, like file 
locations and modules to use.  If you were missing one of the 
more-or-less standard modules to apache, like the MIME determination 
modules, it may not have been able to guess the file types and so served 
plaintext.

2. When you found /etc/rc1.d/KNNhttpd, you found the runlevel 1 (rc1.d) 
non-start (kill -- "K") in alphanumeric order ("NN") script for httpd. 
Each runlevel (Redhat as a server usually uses 3, debian 2) has a rc.d 
directory with symlinks to the main init directory (generally 
/etc/rc.d/init.d), and the naming of the symlinks determines what is 
done on startup.  "K" services are not started.  "S" services are 
started in NN order with "start" as first argument.  The "K" services, 
however, are still symlinks to the /etc/rc.d/init.d scripts that 
actually start and stop things -- only the first letter of the symlink 
name is different -- which is why you were able to run that script with 
"start" to get up and running again.

If you have services that need to start in order, you use the NN numbers 
to order them -- notice that SNNnetworking is before SMMhttpd, since 
httpd needs networking up to run.

Redhat has something called "chkconfig" which I believe handles this 
stuff, if you're not into organizing the symlinks by hand.

Starting a service in this manner doesn't give any guarantees that it 
will be restarted if it crashes, IIRC.  You may want to look at a tool 
published by DJB that does just that, if things get too crashprone.

You may also want to examine your uptime and runlevel.  If your computer 
was rebooted lately and went into a runlevel where the init script was 
"K" -- "do not start" -- then that may explain things.  Even the apache 
from an old Red Hat should be stable enough not to mysteriously quit 
under no load.  Apache error log *may* have more to say about it as well.

Best,

Randall

-- 
Randall Lucas
Tercent, Inc / SuperSurvey Online Surveys
http://www.supersurvey.com

From steve at wirex.net  Tue Nov  9 23:21:14 2004
From: steve at wirex.net (Steve Beattie)

Subject: [PLUG] Heterogeneous RAID on a Compaq 1800R

	<1100028812.26003.1.camel@denk.nakedape.priv>

On Tue, Nov 09, 2004 at 11:33:32AM -0800, Wil Cooley wrote:
> On Tue, 2004-11-09 at 11:18 -0800, Colin Kuskie wrote:
> > I have a Compaq 1800R that I use to host my church's website.  It has
> > a pair of 4.7 Gb drives with RAID 1 running in the hardware-based RAID
> > hot-swappable drive bay.
> > 
> > Through two very generous donations, I've gotten a pair of 9 Gb and
> > 18Gb drives.  I'd love to be able to use all 4 drives in the same
> > configuration that I currently have, with RAID 1 across the drives
> > for a total of 27 Gb.  And maybe have the extra 4.7 Gb to house
> > /tmp and the swap space.

You're looking for RAID 1+0 (aka 10) support (generally, you don't want
the similar RAID 0+1). -ENOCLUE if your specific hardware supports that,
you'll need to play around with the RAID bios or look in the users manual,
if you can find it. The only manual I could find was for an 1850R, and
if it's to be believed, the only RAID configs it supports are 0, 1, 4,
and 5. You could also do the 3 RAID 1 images in hardware, and then use
software RAID 0 to get your 27 GB image.

> If you use software RAID it is possible, because it just uses
> partitions.  It's very unlikely to work with hardware RAID, because the
> controllers usually want control over the whole disk.

Eh wot? Hardware controllers certainly exist that support RAID 1+0;
controlling partitions versus the whole disk doesn't affect that.  Whether
his hardware does or not all depends on the quality of the RAID chipset.
Mind you, I find software RAID to be more flexible to use and administer.

From creswick at gmail.com  Wed Nov 10 08:37:54 2004
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Wed, 10 Nov 2004 16:37:54 +0000
Subject: [PLUG] Open files
In-Reply-To: <20041110161922.GA12034@themontagnes.com>
References: <20041110161922.GA12034@themontagnes.com>
Message-ID: <c834ec80411100837551ce01e@mail.gmail.com>

On Wed, 10 Nov 2004 08:19:22 -0800, Michael Montagne
<michael at themontagnes.com> wrote:
> So, I'm switching my main office file server to Linux/Samba.  One of
> the features I need is the one that list which users have which files
> open.  the lsof command gives too much info and doesn't seem to list
> this sort of info anyway (I think).  Any suggestions?

lsof | grep REG    will filter out a lot of the info you don't want.
lsof <filename>  will only return info about that file, likewise:
lsof /usr/bin/*  ,for example will do what you would expect, so
combining find and some other magic, a line like this should do it:

find <root of interest> <possible file mask(s) w/ find magic> | xargs lsof 

eg:
find / -name "*.txt" | xargs lsof 
will give you all users with .txt files (and those files) open on your system

add some perl...

find / -name "*.txt" | xargs lsof  | 
perl -e 'foreach(<>){@fields = split(/\s+/); print "$fields[2] $fields[8]\n";}'

to get just the username & file (all one line--and there has to be a
shorter sollution...)  I'm sure someone with a bigger perl hat can
easily work in a uniqueness check, so you only get one line / user.

HTH,
-Rogan

From heinlein at madboa.com  Wed Nov 10 08:47:24 2004
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Wed, 10 Nov 2004 08:47:24 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Open files
In-Reply-To: <20041110161922.GA12034@themontagnes.com>
References: <20041110161922.GA12034@themontagnes.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0411100835440.4957@pecos.madboa.com>

On Wed, 10 Nov 2004, Michael Montagne wrote:

> So, I'm switching my main office file server to Linux/Samba.  One of 
> the features I need is the one that list which users have which 
> files open.  the lsof command gives too much info and doesn't seem 
> to list this sort of info anyway (I think).  Any suggestions?

The +D option in lsof will limit your search to a specific directory 
or set of directories:

   lsof +D /home

In a pipeline, it's fairly easy to get a list of user names and open 
files:

   lsof +D /home | grep -w REG | awk '{print $3" "$9}'

-- Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com>

On Wed, Nov 10, 2004 at 07:47:03AM -0800, Wil Cooley wrote:
> Hmm... yeah.  I wasn't thinking of 1+0; I was thinking of doing 5 across
> 4 9-G partitions.  1+0 would only give him 18G; 5 would give him 27G and
> a hot spare.

RAID 1+0 could give him 27GB as well: one RAID 1 set across the 18GB
drives, and one RAID 1 set across the 9 GB drives; then RAID 0 (or I
suppose LVM) across those RAID 1 sets to get the full 27GB. As far as
I understand it, there's no requirement that all the disks in RAID 1+0
all be the same size. Though again, whether a hardware based chipset
will support that configuration depends on the implementation, but
software RAID under linux will allow this.

From heinlein at madboa.com  Wed Nov 10 09:54:21 2004
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Wed, 10 Nov 2004 09:54:21 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Directory size
In-Reply-To: <4192533A.8020306@linuxlink.com>
References: <4192533A.8020306@linuxlink.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0411100948210.17962@pecos.madboa.com>

On Wed, 10 Nov 2004, Michael H. Collins wrote:

> When I was using /var/spool/mail it was easy to determine who had how much 
> mail.  Now I am using Maildir which puts it in ones /home/foo/Maildir/new 
> directory.
>
> What would be the simplest way to find out and list who has how much mail?

# how much unread mail in general inbox
du -sh /home/*/Maildir/new

# how much read mail in general inbox
du -sh /home/*/Maildir/cur

# how much total mail: inbox + all folders
du -sh /home/*/Maildir

-- Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com>

Yeah, hardware RAID never as flexible as software.

From mel97215 at comcast.net  Wed Nov 10 16:34:11 2004
From: mel97215 at comcast.net (Mel Andres)
Date: Wed, 10 Nov 2004 16:34:11 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Sharing calendar data with Thunderbird and Firefox
	extensions.
Message-ID: <4192B383.6080902@comcast.net>

Suse 9.0 Personal
Thunderbird 0.9
Firefox 1.0

After installing the calendar extension for both my browser and email 
client, I wanted to be able to share the data. That is, I didn't want to 
have to enter my appointments twice. I found that the data is contained 
in a file called CalendarDataFile.ics. Each application has this file 
initially located in ~/.mozilla/firefox/default.g9k/Calendar for the 
browser, and ~/.thunderbird/default.rvo/Calendar for the mail client. As 
I had first set up my calendar in Firefox, I wanted the same schedule 
information available when I brought up the calendar in Thunderbird. I 
first deleted ~/.thunderbird/default.rvo/Calendar/CalendarDataFile.ics, 
then while still in ~/.thunderbird/default.rvo/Calendar, I created a 
symbolic link to 
~/.mozilla/firefox/default.g9k/Calendar/CalendarDataFile.ics.

ln -s 
/home/mel/.mozilla/firefox/default.g9k/Calendar/CalendarDataFile.ics 
CalendarDataFile.ics

Viola! I now see the same appointments, in either calendar.

From 1s7k8uhcd001 at sneakemail.com  Wed Nov 10 17:08:35 2004
From: 1s7k8uhcd001 at sneakemail.com (Steve Bonds)
Date: Wed, 10 Nov 2004 17:08:35 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Stack location variance on RH9.0, not on RH8.0
Message-ID: <31122-24646@sneakemail.com>

Folks:

I'm in the process of writing a paper on a linux stack overflow exploit,
and one of the things I noticed when running the exploit against a Red Hat
9.0 system is the return address to the exploit code was impossible to
nail down to the degree of precision needed.

However, on Red Hat 8.0 the needed return address was very consistent.  I
finally reduced the problem to an inconsistent stack location on a default
(unpatched) install of Red Hat 9.0.  The cheezy sample code attached will
display the stack pointer based on the address of a newly allocated local
variable.  (Is there a better way to find the current value of the stack
pointer in C?)

When run on Red Hat 8.0, I see:

-----
$ for i in 1 2 3 4 5; do ./stackp; done
&sp is 0xbffffb44
&sp is 0xbffffb44
&sp is 0xbffffb44
&sp is 0xbffffb44
&sp is 0xbffffb44
-----

However, when run on Red Hat 9.0, I see:

-----
$ for i in 1 2 3 4 5; do ./stackp; done
&sp is 0xbffff8c4
&sp is 0xbffff844
&sp is 0xbffff744
&sp is 0xbffff644
&sp is 0xbffff5c4
-----

There's a definite pattern to the location of the stack pointer on Red Hat
9, but I don't know why it wouldn't be the same as Red Hat 8.  Was this a
security feature?  Unintended consequences of some other change?  The
locations aren't random enough for this to be a result of one of the many
stack protection schemes out there.

My googling led me to the same question, without any definitive answer:

http://www.linuxquestions.org/questions/showthread.php?threadid=148002

Anyone know why Red Hat 9.0 does this?

  -- Steve
-------------- next part --------------
/* stackp.c

   This program prints the current stack pointer upon initialization.

   By Steve Bonds
   Nov 3 2004

   No copyright claimed, this code is Public Domain
   http://creativecommons.org/licenses/publicdomain/

*/

#include <stdio.h>
#include <stdlib.h>

int main(int argc, char *argv[]) {
  int sp;

  printf("&sp is %p\n",&sp);

  exit(0);
}

> 
> http://www.linuxquestions.org/questions/showthread.php?threadid=148002

> Anyone know why Red Hat 9.0 does this?

Wild guess: prelink?

prelink(8)
...
-R --random
     When assigning addresses to libraries, start with random address
     within architecture dependant virtual address space range.  This
     can  make  some  buffer  overflow  attacks  slightly  harder  to
     exploit, because libraries are not present on the same addresses
     accross  different  machines.    Normally,   assigning   virtual
     addresses  starts at the bottom of architecture dependant range.

On Thu, 11 Nov 2004, Vincent Yau wrote:

> Is there a way to choose booting into command-line login? I am using
> kernel 2.6.9.  Right now, it defaults into GNOME login.

   Modify /etc/inittab to reflect the default runlevel of 3.

# Default runlevel. (Do not set to 0 or 6)
id:3:initdefault:

> prelink(8)

Unfortunately, I haven't used prelink.  I also see the same behavior
when I statically link the sample code in my original message, which
eliminates a lot of ldd-strangeness possibilities:

------
$ gcc -Wall -g -o stackp.static --static stackp.c
$ file stackp.static
stackp.static: ELF 32-bit LSB executable, Intel 80386, version 1
(SYSV), for GNU/Linux 2.2.5, statically linked, not stripped
$ for i in 1 2 3 4 5; do ./stackp.static; done
&sp is 0xbfff76e4
&sp is 0xbfffa964
&sp is 0xbfffa694
&sp is 0xbfffc364
&sp is 0xbfffb2f4
-----

The fact that Red Hat 9 does this is a good thing from a security
perspective, but a bad thing from a security research perspective. 
;-)

  -- Steve


From keithl at kl-ic.com  Thu Nov 11 13:15:21 2004
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Thu, 11 Nov 2004 13:15:21 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Dead Knoppix Society
Message-ID: <20041111211520.GA27787@gate.kl-ic.com>


It turns out I have been passing out some bad Knoppix disks.  
I verify every disk after writing it, but some have gone gunnysack
in the field.  Bit rot?  

The disks are Memorex brand, identifiable by a inner label starting
with "LH3513 HJ042 ____ __" .  If you have recently gotten a KNOPPIX 3.6A
disk from me,  please test it with a "knoppix testcd" after the boot
prompt.  If it is flaky, I will swap with you at the next meeting.

Is it dead, or is it Memorex?  Unfortunately, some of these places use
cheap Indian manufactured disk-du-jour, and sometimes they are great and
sometimes they are junk.  Worst case is when they fall apart a long time
after writing.

To date, I have had the best luck with Fuji media.  I have had bad luck
with TDK, Dysan, Imation, Verbatim, HP, and cheap-store-brand. I'm still
testing Sony.  Knoppix disks write all the way out to the edge, and the
"good zone" for some of these disks may be confined to the first 600MB
or so.  I write the disks with Liteon 52X drives, two at a time.

Keith

Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

Subject: [PLUG] Network question
Message-ID: <1100279979.5026.36.camel@einstein.seansdomain.org>

I need to test latency and packet loss on specific router interfaces
(about 10,000 interfaces).  My plan is to select a random device on the
interface and ping it 10 times, and storing the best, worst, average and
packet loss in a database table.  The issue is that this will be done
from a server on a WAN, not the local router/bridge.  

So I'm trying to figure out how to minimize the WAN portion of the ping.
Is it valid to also ping the router and subtract the difference?  I'm
wondering about the validity of this because 1) Network conditions
always change 2) Router's can be configured to respond to pings at a
very low priority.

Also, what's the best way to "ping" or detect latency/packet loss?  Is
it ping or is there a better tool?  What's the best tool to do this in
Perl.

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Fri Nov 12 09:42:53 2004
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Fri, 12 Nov 2004 09:42:53 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Postfix mailq
In-Reply-To: <20041112173217.79113.qmail@web53608.mail.yahoo.com>
References: <20041112173217.79113.qmail@web53608.mail.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <20041112174252.GB30763@rheingold.nakedape.priv>

Also Sprach Scott Van Hoosen <svanhoosen at yahoo.com> on Fri, Nov 12, 2004 at 09:32:17AM PST:
> Two Postfix servers that I take care of, both on Red Hat/Fedora, queue
> up attempts at relaying through my servers. I would like Postfix to
> simply discard e-mail not addressed to my domain, but it seems to try
> to bounce these back to the sender, which of course are always forged.
> I haven't been able to find the setting to change this by Googling. Any
> ideas?
> 
> If this is helpful, here's a section of output from mailq:
> 
> F201B1EE38C     3832 Fri Nov 12 06:58:31  svanhoosen
>         (connect to mail2.saveinternet.net[69.42.112.7]: Connection
> timed out)
>                                          dallasburchfield at weabc.net

This message, while it seems to lack your domain, appears to be
addressed to you.  Postfix generally don't accept messages for domains
that it aren't part of $mydestination or $relay_domains, unless they come
from $mynetworks or through SMTP auth.  It's possible you've
mis-configured your server.  What does your
'smtpd_recipient_restrictions' look like, or better yet, your 'postconf
-n'?

Wil

Scott,

   I assume that in /etc/postfix/main.cf you have
local_recipient_maps = unix:passwd.byname $alias_maps

Is this correct?

   I see reports every day from pflogsumm that attempts to non-existant users
were rejected. Are you trying to discard them instead? I'd be inclined, in
the latter case, to ask for directions or guidance from the mailhelp list.

>   I have SpamAssassin-2.64 limping along here. It was installed using CPAN.

I'm not sure what the difference is between 'install' and 'upgrade' on
Slackware; on RPM it just means it permits installing replacement
files that would otherwise conflict.

BTW, make sure SA finds your Net::DNS working; mine didn't by default
(not sure why...), so I had to set 'dns_available yes' and it makes a
hell of a difference--the SURBLs (http://www.surbl.org/) really add
points.  (Now that I actually look at that site, I probably need to
update my Net::DNS...)  I also recommend installing DCC, Pyzor, Razor2,
Mail::SPF::Query and add a Postgrey greylisting server...

Of course, if you were using RHEL3 or a clone, I could point you at my
Yum repo... http://haus.nakedape.cc/ftp/nakedape/rhel3/

Wil

From Steve.Daggett at fiserv.com  Fri Nov 12 10:02:27 2004
From: Steve.Daggett at fiserv.com (Daggett, Steve)
Date: Fri, 12 Nov 2004 10:02:27 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Network question
Message-ID: <008A26A81DAD96489E7D4FCF259849982D40D1@eft01ms01.eft.fiserv.net>

 
Sean Whitney wrote:
> 
> I need to test latency and packet loss on specific router 
> interfaces (about 10,000 interfaces).  My plan is to select a 
> random device on the interface and ping it 10 times, and 
> storing the best, worst, average and packet loss in a 
> database table.  The issue is that this will be done from a 
> server on a WAN, not the local router/bridge.  

  That's a lot-o-interfaces (tm) to ping.  You're immediately going to have
a problem parallelizing the pings with most ping utilities.  I currently use
a home grown Cricket script to measure latency in my network.  Cricket sucks
at latency measurement because it's unable to parallelize the tasks.
Cricket will only latency check a few interfaces before it runs out of time.


> Also, what's the best way to "ping" or detect latency/packet 
> loss?  Is it ping or is there a better tool?  What's the best 
> tool to do this in Perl.

  I'd recommend looking at Tobias Oetiker's "Smokeping" utility.  It already
has a lot of the capabilities you're looking for.  There are a number of
plugins which use telnet or rsh to ping from a remote host or router.  

http://people.ee.ethz.ch/~oetiker/webtools/smokeping/

  If you're committed to rolling your own solution, look at "fping",
"echoping", and "curl".  Those utils will help you monitor almost anything
in your network.  The Smokeping plugins are a good example of how the utils
can be used.  There are also some perl modules that do the same things, but
I'll leave that to someone more monk'ish than me.  

Steve D...

From AthlonRob at axpr.net  Fri Nov 12 10:49:12 2004
From: AthlonRob at axpr.net (AthlonRob)
Date: Fri, 12 Nov 2004 10:49:12 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Postfix mailq
In-Reply-To: <20041112182213.4750.qmail@web53607.mail.yahoo.com>
References: <20041112182213.4750.qmail@web53607.mail.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <1100285353.815.23.camel@dell.linux.box>

On Fri, 2004-11-12 at 10:22 -0800, Scott Van Hoosen wrote:
> I just went through my maillog and found some of the messages stuck in
> my mailq. They are addressed to random nonexistant users in my domain.
> Postfix seems to want to bounce replies back to the forged sender.
> Sorry I didn't have this info in my original post. Anyway I would like
> Postfix to stop trying to bounce these back to the sender, but is that
> a bad idea because someone might misspell a real username?

That's why most of us reject mail addressed to unknown local users.
Leave the burden on the connecting mail server; if it's a virus or
spammer of some sort, no bounce will be generated.  If it's a legitimate
mail server, *it* will have the responsibility of generating a bounce.

Also Sprach Scott Van Hoosen <svanhoosen at yahoo.com> on Fri, Nov 12, 2004 at 10:22:13AM PST:
> I just went through my maillog and found some of the messages stuck in
> my mailq. They are addressed to random nonexistant users in my domain.
> Postfix seems to want to bounce replies back to the forged sender.
> Sorry I didn't have this info in my original post. Anyway I would like
> Postfix to stop trying to bounce these back to the sender, but is that
> a bad idea because someone might misspell a real username?

Well, that's different than messages addressed to other domains.  Rich's
message has the solution to that; you'll need to add $virtual_maps and/or
$virtual_mailbox_maps.

Also, the svanhoosen is the sender of message in the queue because you set
'empty_address_recipient' to it; I'd recommend disabling that, I think.
I don't know what harm would actually come of it, but it looks odd.
(Of course make sure your aliases points MAILER-DAEMON at the right user;
the stock aliases file from Postfix does everything correctly if you just
set root's alias.)

From 1s7k8uhcd001 at sneakemail.com  Fri Nov 12 15:23:11 2004
From: 1s7k8uhcd001 at sneakemail.com (Steve Bonds)
Date: Fri, 12 Nov 2004 15:23:11 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] ssh tunnels
In-Reply-To: <20041112220150.GA9075@subtend.net>
References: <20041112220150.GA9075@subtend.net>
Message-ID: <20842-82441@sneakemail.com>

On Fri, 12 Nov 2004 14:01:50 -0800, Kris krisa-at-subtend.net |PDX
Linux| <...> wrote:
> I'm trying to understand the difference between these two SSH
> commands, the end goal is to tunnel localport 2525 to a remote
> server's 25 to allow me to relay mail out the server.
> 
> This doesn't work (but builds a tunnel just fine):
> ssh -f -L 2525:host.dom:25 host.dom -N
> 
> This works great:
> ssh -f _l 2525:host.dom:25 -N localhost

I think your syntax on the second one might be off-- what did you really mean?

Your first one should have worked, though I usually write it as:

ssh -N -L 2525:localhost:25 host.dom

This means:

-N: don't run a command, just open the tunnel
-L 2525: open port 2525 on the system you're running ssh from
    localhost: connect the above port to this host from the host I'm
about to ssh into
    25: on this port
localhost: connect to this host and form the connection to the -L host from here

Most of the time the host you want in your tunnel option (-L/-R) is
"localhost" unless you're doing something really odd.

  -- Steve

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Fri Nov 12 13:32:31 2004
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (Darkhorse)

Subject: [PLUG] Postfix mailq
In-Reply-To: <20041112173217.79113.qmail@web53608.mail.yahoo.com>

On Fri, 2004-11-12 at 09:32, Scott Van Hoosen wrote:
> Two Postfix servers that I take care of, both on Red Hat/Fedora, queue
> up attempts at relaying through my servers. I would like Postfix to
> simply discard e-mail not addressed to my domain, but it seems to try
> to bounce these back to the sender, which of course are always forged.
> I haven't been able to find the setting to change this by Googling. Any
> ideas?
> 
> If this is helpful, here's a section of output from mailq:
> 
> F201B1EE38C     3832 Fri Nov 12 06:58:31  svanhoosen

>                  (connect to pjn.qsrch.net[64.94.29.14]: Connection
> timed out)

>  
> F1C7A1EE2CD     3631 Thu Nov 11 10:22:43  svanhoosen
>            (connect to a.mx.emlcampaign.com[63.79.4.17]: Connection
> timed out)
>                                         
> brilliantmarketinginc at emlcampaign.com
>  
> F422A1EE3A1     3787 Fri Nov 12 07:30:53  svanhoosen
>         (connect to mail1.saveinternet.net[69.42.112.4]: Connection
> timed out)
>                                         
> olengaston at domainsecurefirst.com
> 
> 
> -Scott
> 
Some email sources aren't dns listed and you just have to accept them
explicitly.  It would be interesting if you could bypass just the helo
checking for certain helo names, but I don't know how to do that.

Go ahead and helo check.  I recommend what's called a relay 
firewall in front of your mail server.  Check out postfix.org 
for a config that only relays listed recipients.  I'm attaching
my own config.  Helo checking can block legitimate email.  I 
strongly suggest a script that checks the mail log on the relay
for messages to valid recipients that have been rejected using
the mail command to notify them.

I wrote such a script which emails the valid recipient with
the information about the rejection in case there needs
to be an exception for a certain message source.  Another option
I haven't played with much is a primary relay that helo
checks with a secondary that doesn't.  Better have a tertiary
set up as spamtrap for people who expect the secondary to be
more permissive and consistently go to it first incorrectly. 

All legitimate email servers are supposed to retry at least once.
Problems arise with sites that don't dns list their outgoing mail
servers such as AOL.  It may make their security easier, but it's
a pain for everyone else.

I've attached my main.cf for a postfix 2.1.1 gateway relay.
If you look carefully you'll notice sections for spf and
greylisting as well.  When you use greylisting it's an
especially good idea to have a notifictation script that
runs at least once a day so that your users can find out
about legitimate email attempts to them from sources that 
aren't greylisting compatible.

Your main source of course is postfix.org.

Another thing I've done is write another script that drops ip addresses
of abusers into an iptables chain.  I drop anyone who uses an invalid 
recipient name immediately dropping people who abuse smtp command
pipelining or come in with a hostname that's unknown for three days
after there have been three instances of abuse.

My rejection rate is hovering at 25% or so now down from 70-90%.
To stop bouncing use the luser relay feature.  The only problem with
this approach is that you have to do extra work to notify anyone
that should recieve a bounce there has been a problem.

     --  Michael C. Robinson
-------------- next part --------------
queue_directory = /var/spool/relay_spool
command_directory = /usr/sbin
daemon_directory = /usr/libexec/postfix

mail_owner = postfix

default_privs = nobody

mydomain=robinson-west.com

myorigin=$mydomain

inet_interfaces = 209.210.202.170, 127.0.0.1

mydestination=
local_recipient_maps= 
local_transport= error:local delivery is disabled

virtual_alias_maps=hash:/etc/postfix/virtual

mynetworks=127.0.0.0/8, 209.210.202.168/29
relay_domains = $mydomain, goose.$mydomain
parent_domain_matches_subdomains = 
    debug_peer_list smtpd_access_maps

relay_recipient_maps=hash:/etc/postfix/relay_recipients
transport_maps=hash:/etc/postfix/transport



in_flow_delay = 1s

smtpd_banner = $myhostname ESMTP $mail_name

smtpd_delay_reject = yes


smtpd_helo_required=yes

disable_vrfy_command=yes

smtpd_reject_unlisted_sender=yes

relocated_maps=hash:/etc/postfix/relocated


smtpd_helo_restrictions= check_helo_access hash:/etc/postfix/access,
                         check_helo_access hash:/etc/postfix/invalid_helo,
                         reject_invalid_hostname,
                         reject_non_fqdn_hostname,
                         reject_unknown_hostname,
                         permit_mynetworks

smtpd_recipient_restrictions = 
               check_sender_access hash:/etc/postfix/override_sender_access,
                                     reject_non_fqdn_sender,
                                  reject_non_fqdn_recipient,
                               reject_unknown_sender_domain, 
                            reject_unknown_recipient_domain,
                                          permit_mynetworks,
                                  reject_unauth_destination,
                   check_policy_service inet:127.0.0.1:9998,
        check_sender_access hash:/etc/postfix/sender_access,
                                  reject_unlisted_recipient,
                   check_policy_service unix:private/policy,
        check_recipient_access hash:/etc/postfix/postmaster,
                           reject_rbl_client dnsbl.ahbl.org,

From heinlein at madboa.com  Fri Nov 12 20:34:04 2004
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Fri, 12 Nov 2004 20:34:04 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] httpd.conf question
In-Reply-To: <477eba8c04111216541891c49b@mail.gmail.com>
References: <477eba8c04111216541891c49b@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0411122027530.11595@hedwig.cse.ogi.edu>

On Fri, 12 Nov 2004, Bryan Murdock wrote:

> Right now I can drop the script.cgi into /var/www/cgi-bin and access 
> it with this:
>
> http://hostname/cgi-bin/script.cgi
>
> That's fine and great, but it would be nice if someone could just type:
>
> http://hostname

The RedirectMatch directive is your friend:

   RedirectMatch permanent \
     ^/$ \
     http://hostname/cgi-bin/script.cgi

I'd have to play some with the regexes to include invocations of 
/index.htm[l] to work as well, but it'd end up looking something 
like this:

   Redirect Match permanent \
     ^/(index\.html?)?$ \
     http://hostname/cgi-bin/script.cgi

-- Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com>


From clydefrog at adnap.no-ip.com  Fri Nov 12 22:24:22 2004
From: clydefrog at adnap.no-ip.com (Evan Heidtmann)
Date: Fri, 12 Nov 2004 22:24:22 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] httpd.conf question
In-Reply-To: <477eba8c04111216541891c49b@mail.gmail.com>
References: <477eba8c04111216541891c49b@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <1100327062.6840.11.camel@timmy>

On Fri, 2004-11-12 at 16:54, Bryan Murdock wrote:
> I'm pretty much a n00b when it comes to configuring apache.  I just
> want to run a little web server on my linux machine here at work to
> run some perl cgi stuff.  This is on a brand new Fedora Core 3
> install.  Right now I can drop the script.cgi into /var/www/cgi-bin
> and access it with this:
> 
> http://hostname/cgi-bin/script.cgi
> 
> That's fine and great, but it would be nice if someone could just type:
> 
> http://hostname

Paul's suggestion is good if you don't mind the actual URL being
/cgi-bin/script.cgi. If you want the CGI script to act as an index for a
directory, you'll need to do three things:

<Directory /the/place/where/the/cgi/is>
  # This is generally on anyway, but turn it on to be safe
  Options +Indexes 

  # Make files ending in cgi and pl act as CGI scripts in this
  # directory.
  # (/cgi-bin/ uses the different ScriptAlias directive)
  AddHandler cgi-script .cgi .pl

  # Make index.cgi the index file. You can add other filenames here
  # as well.
  DirectoryIndex index.cgi
</Directory>

If you apply these directives to your DocumentRoot (or use 
<Location /> instead of <Directory ...>), you can put index.cgi in your
DocumentRoot and access it with http://hostname.

AddHandler is discussed here:

http://httpd.apache.org/docs/mod/mod_mime.html#addhandler

And DirectoryIndex here:

http://httpd.apache.org/docs/mod/mod_dir.html#directoryindex

Evan

> I've searched all over and I can't find any information on how to 
> generated the repodata subdirectory. The yum-arch utility doesn't do

createrepo is the application you want. A discussion of the drive 
behind the change to repodata is online at Duke:

   http://linux.duke.edu/projects/metadata

You can get createrepo from a number of rpm repositories, or you can 
go to the source:

   http://linux.duke.edu/projects/metadata/generate/

-- Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com>


From aaron at bavariati.org  Sat Nov 13 14:01:59 2004
From: aaron at bavariati.org (Aaron Burt)
Date: Sat, 13 Nov 2004 14:01:59 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] LTSP
In-Reply-To: <20041111025647.58626.qmail@web50601.mail.yahoo.com>
References: <20041111025647.58626.qmail@web50601.mail.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <20041113220159.GD16246@bavariati.org>

On Wed, Nov 10, 2004 at 06:56:47PM -0800, Robert Kopp wrote:
> First, is that what I mean? Suppose you have a rather
> capable Linux computer with an Ethernet connection
> (that is, a unit capable of functioning as a server)
> and you'd like to share these resources with a second
> user, using some kind of "thin client" with its own
> monitor, mouse, and keyboard. This other unit would
> run programs installed on the server.

LTSP is worth the trouble if you want to have a bunch of diskless thin
clients.  Then it works really well.

For your situation, you'd probably want to set up the second computer
with a basic local Linux install, with X.  Then (I think) have the
machine's display manager (XDM, GDM, KDM, etc.) connect to the server
box instead of localhost.

Don't forget to allow remote connections to the thin client's X server.

Have fun,
  Aaron

On Sat, 2004-11-13 at 14:01, Aaron Burt wrote:
> For your situation, you'd probably want to set up the second computer
> with a basic local Linux install, with X.  Then (I think) have the
> machine's display manager (XDM, GDM, KDM, etc.) connect to the server
> box instead of localhost.
> 
> Don't forget to allow remote connections to the thin client's X server.

That sounds complicated. I just set the display manager (I've only used
GDM) to accept XDMCP requests and then run this on the client:

$ X -query the-server

This is using the regular notion of server/client, not X's notion.

You can put that in /etc/inittab to do it on boot and always restart
when killed.

Evan

remove via82cxxx_audio { /usr/sbin/alsactl store >/dev/null 2>&1 || : ; }; 
/sbin/modprobe -r --ignore-remove via82cxxx_audio

free Internet access except for a number of specific

to an ip address from working without setting up a

worried about that.

I am not the authoritative dns server for banana.org.  I'd 
like to be able to acheive something with my own internal
robinson-west.com name server similar to:

bananna.org.	A	10.0.0.2

What I'm after here is you can try to look up bananna.org,
but the nameserver for bananna.org will never be queried 
and my local nameserver will resolve your query to 10.0.0.2.  
At 10.0.0.2 I put up a webserver that says, "you're attempt 
to go to bananna.org was denied."  I don't want to break 
all foreign sites.  Even though bananna.org doesn't resolve 
correctly, ebay.com etc. still should.  Instead of just 
iptables blocking certain ip addresses, I want to
avoid the problem of hanging people's web browsers.



What I'm after can be done with /etc/hosts in this way:

10.0.0.2	bananna.org

I don't want an ugly /etc/hosts file though and this 
doesn't affect workstations that use the dns.



The main  reason I'm looking for a don't look it up 
solution is that I do upwards of a 1000 host lookups 
as it is to block certain web sites using iptables.  
Web sites can ip fluctuate, especially if they are 
set up on a dial-up line or a random ip in a large 
netblock.  

One reason I don't want to look offensive sites up 
is that this may suggest interest in offensive 
materials and send the following incorrect message 
out, "here I am, please spam me."

From AthlonRob at axpr.net  Sat Nov 13 23:42:16 2004

> names I don't want to permit accurate lookups for.  If a
