Well... I've been running entirely through squid for the last two and a
half years and have only had one instance of it not working correctly;
it was a Windows Update bug.  *Every* other website has worked fine...

AFAIK, the only way to accomplish this with bind is to have entries in
named.conf for each domain you wish to block.

A transparent squid proxy is really more effective, though... it can
block IPs and hostnames.  If you simply block dns resolution, the user
could easily look up the IP online and browse directly to that....

to work through squid for example and I hear that myfamily.com has
problems too.  Is there special patching to squid that has to be done
for secure web pages, etc.?  If there's a way to get practically any
site to work through squid, that would be better because it would

I can't get windowsupdate to work through squid.  For obvious reasons,
it would nice to force content from windowsupdate.com through antivirus
prior to letting these updates install on a workstation.  I don't think
ports 80 and 443 are the only tcp/udp ports that have to be open for
windowsupdate.com to work.  I'm so frustrated with not being able to run
windowsupdate.com through a filtered proxy that I've almost said no
Windows machines on the Internet.  The only Windows system I can get
my users to agree with me on for that policy is Windows XP.  I don't
know that you can get myfamily.com to work without Windows 98 or 2000
and Internet Explorer.  Probably the smartest thing a person could
do is have the Windows installed to a ram drive and run that system
so that there's no concern about booting a virused Windows machine. 
This might be accomplished by replacing the Windows entirely before 
each session from a known good copy and using a protected 
(non flash) bios.  It's surprising the latter isn't standard on
new computers, even a switch or jumper to disable bios upgrades
isn't standard.  Would it be that hard to have a power, reset, 
and bios upgrade switch?  I'd turn that third switch off when
I'm online.  

Another option is running the web browser chroot, but I believe that
only Mac OSX 10.3, Linux, and other Unix systems support this.  It 
would be nice to have a howto on how to make users use mozilla in a
chroot sandbox built in their home directory.  I'm also curios about
forcing deletion of all cached content in mozilla when any user exits it
under Linux.  How do you allow changing to the skypilot theme without
allowing cached content to persist across multiple instantiations
of mozilla?  I'd probably need to teach people to save their bookmarks
to a persistent location.  I'm more concerned there may be important
cookies and that it may not be clear what's in them.  Usually you
want to dump all of your cookies, but with sites like myfamily.com
this can prove to be a real nuisance policy.  What I'm tryhing to get
away from via chroot is the web browser being a vulnerability to the
underlying operating system.

     --  Michael C. Robinson

From lowbassman at gmail.com  Sun Nov 14 15:11:47 2004
From: lowbassman at gmail.com (Matt Alexander)
Date: Sun, 14 Nov 2004 16:11:47 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Hacking and Universities...
In-Reply-To: <1100465230.10821.79.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
References: <1100465230.10821.79.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <9e0a35780411141511636e59e6@mail.gmail.com>

On 14 Nov 2004 12:47:10 -0800, Darkhorse <plug_0 at robinson-west.com> wrote:
> Has anyone ever heard of network administrators at U of P or faculty
> there intentionally hacking the computers of students?  I've heard
> this might be done to improve security and impart valuable lessons
> to those students who are in the engineering programs.  I suppose
> it's just as likely that this is the work of a random hacker, but
> I'm certainly curious.  The implications for not only plugging
> in at U of P but perhaps other Universities as well could be
> enormous.
> 
> Does anyone know of a $25 easily transported hardware firewall that
> can go between a potentially vulnerable laptop or computer and
> a University's network?  This would ideally be a cube no thicker
> than 4 leather wallets stacked with an ethernet port for the
> student's laptop and another ethernet port for the University's
> network.  If such a product isn't commonly available, it might
> be worth going into business to make one.  I'd rather see a $25
> hardware firewall hacked instead of seeing my $1k+ computer taken
> out.  I can buy a lot of $25 computers before I'm up to the cost
> of replacing one $1k+ computer.

Yes, unfortunately this is a growing issue across the U.S. at quite a
few universities.  This has been covered in the news in several campus
newspapers (some students were skilled enough to figure out what was
going on), but unfortunately it hasn't received any mainstream media
attention yet.  Basically it goes like this...  Engineering students
are being targeted by their instructors to "teach them a lesson" about
computer security they'll never forget.  If these students are going
to be designing the systems of tomorrow then they had better damn well
understand computer security, for the sake of us all.

Usually it progresses in 3 phases.  The first phase is just access to
the student's computer and the deletion of a few harmless files or
other activities that make the system perform strangely (this may have
happened to you already).  If the student still hasn't properly
secured their computer then phase two is implemented.  Phase 2
involves the installation of programs designed to make the use of the
computer frustrating to use - constant popups, false virus warnings,
random program crashes, etc.  Phase 2 will often times involve the
installation of a root kit for attacking other students' systems and
to hide the instructor's origin.  Phase 3 wipes the filesystem of all
data.

These university instructors are excellent hackers and they not only
know the university's systems inside and out, they are usually
specialists in a particular OS and often work in teams against more
challenging targets.
Your idea of a 4-leather-wallet-high firewall is a good one, but that
might not even be enough.  These university instructors can be
ruthless.  You might need 2 or even 3 firewalls just to be on the safe
side.
Good luck!


From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Sun Nov 14 16:18:04 2004
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (Darkhorse)

On Sun, 2004-11-14 at 15:11, Bill Thoen wrote:

> - Bill Thoen
> 
> _______________________________________________
> PLUG mailing list
> PLUG at lists.pdxlinux.org
> http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug

There appears to be a serious security flaw in the G4 iBook.  It could
be that Mac OS 9 is totally insecure, my brother's iBook was running
OS/9 when the virus hit.  A hardware firewall/filter proxy cube might
protect you if you don't have your latop or desktop firewall configured 
correctly.  The virus that hit my brother's laptop turned it 
into a multicast broadcaster to who knows here.  This
virus got past his antiviral software and it survived a zero fill
reformatting of the hard drive.  It must be in the bios of the
computer.  Short of being able to pull and reprogram or replace 
the bios chip, there's no way I can see to get rid of this virus. 
That's assuming there isn't a bug in Mac OSX 10.3's format program 
that is allowing the virus to survive the format operation.

Why apple and other computer manufacturers don't offer a 
physical bios upgrade prevention switch or jumper block
I can't tell you.  Computers that have no backup to their
flash bioses can be rendered useless by viruses.  It used to
be that you'd pull the bios chip out of a socket and buy
a replacement.  Many bioses now are surface mount
flash chips, so there's no easy fix.  Surface mount flash
with no protective jumpers may be cheaper to manufacture,
but this cost cutting measure sucks from a security 
standpoint.

If there's something wrong with the tcp/ip stack on the G4 iBook,
a firewall cube set up properly could protect against hackers
trying to take advantage of this weakness.

Can you replace the G4 iBook's boot code without OS/9?
Can the bios chip be removed and replaced?

My brother had ipfw configured.  I don't know if his firewall 
was up when the virus hit.  Apparently it came in through
Safari.  He believes that the virus gets around ipfw 
considering that he has rules denying multicast packets
and his laptop does multicasting to who knows where.
Assuming ipfw on his iBook doesn't work because of a flaw somewhere,
a hardware firewall could indeed be better than the supplied
software one.  This virus eats data files, so his laptop is 
worthless unless it can be cleaned.

Anyone who has an iBook that doesn't want it rendered useless, get
OS/9 off of it ASAP.  Don't have OS/9 installed unless you're 
offline and need to upgrade your bios.  Personally I'd dump
OSX 10.3 and replace it with Mk-Linux, OSX-10.3 obviosuly has
serious security flaws.

    --  Michael C. Robinson



From fpikus at gmail.com  Sun Nov 14 16:10:24 2004
From: fpikus at gmail.com (Fedor Pikus)
Date: Sun, 14 Nov 2004 16:10:24 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Hardware vs Software Firewalls (was: Hacking and
	Universities...)
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.44.0411141602280.16359-100000@gisnet.com>
References: <1100465230.10821.79.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.44.0411141602280.16359-100000@gisnet.com>
Message-ID: <a4fbdc7b041114161033d794cd@mail.gmail.com>

On Sun, 14 Nov 2004 16:11:09 -0700 (MST), Bill Thoen <bthoen at gisnet.com> wrote:
> On 14 Nov 2004, Darkhorse wrote:
> 
> > Does anyone know of a $25 easily transported hardware firewall that
> > can go between a potentially vulnerable laptop or computer and
> > a University's network?
> 
> Why is a separate hardware firewall for a single machine better than a
> software firewall on that single machine? Don't they both block packets as
> instructed? Is there something inherently unsafe about using a software
> firewall?
In general, it's less safe to use the firewall on the PC which also
has to do other stuff than a dedicated firewall. The reason primarily
has to do with the necessary access. If a PC runs more than a
firewall, it has to allow some access (unless the "other stuff" has
nothing to do with networking). Access means potential exploits. If
you browse web from your firewall, the browser could have exploits,
root escalation may be possible, then the firewall can be disabled.
Same idea for web server, nameserver, etc. If the firewall is
dedicated, you could disable all forms of remote access if you wanted,
or restrict them as much as you want.
In practice, the difference targets a fairly narrow range of attacks:
most attackers are not skilled enough to break either firewall, of
those who are most will break any firewall if they really want to, so
with a dedicated firewall you're protecting yourself from those of
"medium" skill, or those of "high" skill who will take one look and
decide that cracking your firewall takes more than 10 seconds and
you're not worth the hassle, and move to the next target.
-- 
Fedor G Pikus (fpikus at gmail.com)
http://www.pikus.net
http://wild-light.com

Software is infinitely more flexible than hardware. _Well_ written
firewall software will clobber hardware any day. All of the "hardware"
firewalls are simply hardware designed to assist firewall software
anyway. Having it in a separate box simply means that you've got firewall
in a separate box, any of the free Unicies will do a firewalling job
equivalent to these "hardware firewalls" easily. If you're on EvilOS
though you don't have any options.

From bmurdock at gmail.com  Mon Nov 15 11:34:25 2004

In-Reply-To: <1100327062.6840.11.camel@timmy>
References: <477eba8c04111216541891c49b@mail.gmail.com>
	<1100327062.6840.11.camel@timmy>
Message-ID: <477eba8c041115113418cf65c5@mail.gmail.com>

On Fri, 12 Nov 2004 22:24:22 -0800, Evan Heidtmann
<clydefrog at adnap.no-ip.com> wrote:
> On Fri, 2004-11-12 at 16:54, Bryan Murdock wrote:
> 
> 
> > I'm pretty much a n00b when it comes to configuring apache.  I just
> > want to run a little web server on my linux machine here at work to
> > run some perl cgi stuff.  This is on a brand new Fedora Core 3
> > install.  Right now I can drop the script.cgi into /var/www/cgi-bin
> > and access it with this:
> >
> > http://hostname/cgi-bin/script.cgi
> >
> > That's fine and great, but it would be nice if someone could just type:
> >
> > http://hostname
> 
> Paul's suggestion is good if you don't mind the actual URL being
> /cgi-bin/script.cgi. If you want the CGI script to act as an index for a
> directory, you'll need to do three things:
> 
> <Directory /the/place/where/the/cgi/is>
>   # This is generally on anyway, but turn it on to be safe
>   Options +Indexes
> 
>   # Make files ending in cgi and pl act as CGI scripts in this
>   # directory.
>   # (/cgi-bin/ uses the different ScriptAlias directive)
>   AddHandler cgi-script .cgi .pl
> 
>   # Make index.cgi the index file. You can add other filenames here
>   # as well.
>   DirectoryIndex index.cgi
> </Directory>
> 
> If you apply these directives to your DocumentRoot (or use
> <Location /> instead of <Directory ...>), you can put index.cgi in your
> DocumentRoot and access it with http://hostname.
> 
> AddHandler is discussed here:
> 
> http://httpd.apache.org/docs/mod/mod_mime.html#addhandler
> 
> And DirectoryIndex here:
> 
> http://httpd.apache.org/docs/mod/mod_dir.html#directoryindex
> 
> Evan

Awesome, I even learned how to change my document root from
/var/www/html to something I like better.  Thanks!!

Bryan

Try running "yum -y update" instead after adding the following lines
to your yum.conf:

[base]
name=Fedora Core $releasever - $basearch - Base
baseurl=http://limestone.uoregon.edu/ftp/fedora/3/i386/os/

[updates-released]
name=Fedora Core $releasever - $basearch - Released Updates
baseurl=http://limestone.uoregon.edu/ftp/fedora/updates/3/i386/

From 1s7k8uhcd001 at sneakemail.com  Mon Nov 15 16:36:58 2004
From: 1s7k8uhcd001 at sneakemail.com (Steve Bonds)
Date: Mon, 15 Nov 2004 16:36:58 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Laptop
In-Reply-To: <E1CTdH5-0000CL-00@pop-a065c05.pas.sa.earthlink.net>
References: <E1CTdH5-0000CL-00@pop-a065c05.pas.sa.earthlink.net>
Message-ID: <7920-76139@sneakemail.com>

On Mon, 15 Nov 2004 01:38:06 -0800, Piet van Weel wrote:

> I'm currently in the market for a GOOD laptop.
> 
> I have $2500 available for purchasing the laptop; however, I need to be able
> to:
> 
> 1.      GIS Software
> 2.      AUTOcad
> 3.      Video Editing, etc.
> 4.      Dual Boot SUSE/Win XP Pro

Requirement 1 and 2 mean you'll be wanting a fair amount of RAM
available.  Requirement 3 also requires lots of RAM, but generally
also means you want a DVD writer.  If you need to do any real-time
compression then you also end up needing a fast CPU and hard drive. 
DV-based digital video requires Firewire.

Did I miss any components?  ;-)

SUSE is fairly compatible with all major laptops.  Major compatibility
problems seem to be pretty rare these days.  However, component
compatiblity seems very common-- particularly built-ins like modems,
Ethernet, Firewire, and wireless adapters.

Once you narrow the field a bit, head over to:

http://www.linux-laptop.net/

Here you can see what works and what doesn't.

SUSE also has a good database of compatible systems:

http://www.suse.com/en/business/certifications/certified_hardware/notebook.html

I would suggest from my experience with laptops that you look at:
  1) Lots of RAM, buy more than you need (1GB, 2GB if you can)
  2) CPU speed is not particularly important.  Faster CPU == shorter
battery and hotter lap
  3) Hard drive speed is going to suck compared with desktops
  4) Long battery life under high CPU load (divide the "rating" by 2
to get something realistic)
  5) Get one that's been out for 6 months or so for better driver support
  6) Check that the Firewire port works on Linux

I've had good luck with both Dell and IBM laptops.  (Others have
reported Dell horror stories, but when mine have broken Dell has been
excellent about getting them fixed.)

  -- Steve

 From root at dimstar.net  Mon Nov 15 04:02:09 2004

  Folder: IN.admin                                                      
2770453

 From root at dimstar.net  Tue Nov 16 04:02:11 2004
 Subject: mckinley.dimstar.net 11/16/04:04.02 system check
  Folder: /var/spool/mail/russj                                         
3741585

The rule I have to do this is:

# Root Mail
:0:
* ^From:.*root at .*dimstar\.net
IN.admin

Any ideas?

From russj at dimstar.net  Tue Nov 16 14:55:44 2004
From: russj at dimstar.net (Russ Johnson)
Date: Tue, 16 Nov 2004 14:55:44 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Procmail quits sorting after a while... Solved
In-Reply-To: <419A5560.2080506@dimstar.net>
References: <419A5560.2080506@dimstar.net>
Message-ID: <419A8570.9010506@dimstar.net>

Russ Johnson wrote:

> OK, here's a procmail problem that comes up with my system every 
> month. Monthly, I have a cron job that archives my sorted mail 
> folders. After that happens, everything works swimmingly... until 
> sometime in the middle of the month. Then procmail doesn't sort 
> certain emails that it sorted previously. It only stops sorting some 
> of the mail. It continues to sort most of my mail properly. Is there a 
> file size it can't write to?

OK, I found it... after searching google for awhile.

mailbox_size_limit = 51200000

That's the default for postfix, and it must transfer this limit to 
procmail, since procmail is delivering.

I'm testing right now, but according to the debian mailing list, this is 
the issue.

Russ

> > My brother had ipfw configured.  I don't know if his firewall

> > Safari.  He believes that the virus gets around ipfw

> > software one.  This virus eats data files, so his laptop is 
> > worthless unless it can be cleaned.
> 
> Well, if it isn't up, it isn't worth much now is it? Preventing such
> packets coming in, doesn't prevent you from sending them out. Perhaps
> this is a virus of the common type called "hardware failure"? These virii
> appear to accumulate on old hardware eventually rendering it completely
> useless (completely dead), no one has tracked down who originally created
> these, but it appears the manufacturers try to encourage their spread in
> order to get people to "upgrade" their computers. Apparently there is a
> special firewall called a "warrenty" that stops these from spreading.

I realize a firewall isn't worth beans if it isn't up, the interesting
thing even though there were rules to block outbound multicasts is that
those rules didn't work.

This isn't old hardware, it's a practically brand new G4 powerbook. 
Shame on CompUSA for not updating the patches when they upgraded 
the hard drive if that is in fact why the laptop failed.  It could be
hardware failure, that's why the laptop was taken away from my brother 
and run over to the shop.  My brother is an EE who feels he 
can fix any computer, but we weren't getting what we really needed
from google and you don't blow $12k over a $1k laptop anyways. 

The biggest problem I see is that protecting the firmware is an
afterthought. It's useless to have a "good OS" if you don't protect
the hardware foundations it runs on.  It's arrogant to say that the
firmware in Apple iBooks doesn't get hacked, arrogant and stupid.

This iBook without some kind of firmware protection is too easily 
hacked. Linux is the only system that appears to work correctly on 
it if it can't be fixed.  We did a test with a ppc gentoo minimal
disk and found it wasn't broadcasting those strange packets that it
does when you're running MacOSX.  Schools use dangerous software like
adobe acrobat, powerpoint, and MS-Word.  How are you supposed to look 
at files from these programs if these files can not only virus, but in
some cases completely destroy your computer?  If you can simulate a
computer and protect the underlying hardware, that might not be so bad.

Whoever thinks that MacOSX is more secure than Linux is full of it.

Hopefully, any flaw is a faulty part and not a design mistake on the
part of the makers of iBook.  You would think that these machines would
get tested at the store before they're sold though.

I sure hope that CompUSA can figure out what went wrong and come up with
an affective prevention method that doesn't bar connecting to networks.

I wonder what the probability is that the MacOSX install disk itself is
hacked?

From ian at znark.com  Tue Nov 16 21:42:33 2004
From: ian at znark.com (Ian Burrell)
Date: Tue, 16 Nov 2004 21:42:33 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] [Q] alternatives
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.33.0411161614230.22789-100000@main.cyber-office.net>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.33.0411161614230.22789-100000@main.cyber-office.net>
Message-ID: <419AE4C9.4050904@znark.com>

Roderick A. Anderson wrote:
> I think it was on this list that I first came across the alternatives
> command (RHL/FC/RHEL) and was it neat.  Now I've build a new server
> (actually 6) that is (are) using postfix (installed using yum and the
> repository at ftp.WL0.org) but alternatives --config mta only shows
> sendmail.  Not too much of a problem but several of the postfix
> support-type commands don't work (or not correctly any way).  Is there a
> hand method to get the postfix alternative installed?  My assumption is
> the rpm I got didn't do the things it needed to do to make this work.
> 
> As usual pointers or clue-sticks are appreciated.
> 

If you are using the third-party package of postfix, I bet it didn't run 
the right scripts when it installed.  My FC3 postfix package runs 
alternatives as part of its post-install scripts.  This might do the job 
(be careful with word-wrap).

/usr/sbin/alternatives --install /usr/sbin/sendmail mta 
/usr/sbin/sendmail.postfix 30 \
         --slave /usr/bin/mailq mta-mailq /usr/bin/mailq.postfix \
         --slave /usr/bin/newaliases mta-newaliases 
/usr/bin/newaliases.postfix \        --slave /etc/pam.d/smtp mta-pam 
/etc/pam.d/smtp.postfix \
         --slave /usr/bin/rmail mta-rmail /usr/bin/rmail.postfix \
         --slave /usr/lib/sendmail mta-sendmail /usr/lib/sendmail.postfix \
         --slave /usr/share/man/man1/mailq.1.gz mta-mailqman 
/usr/share/man/man1/mailq.postfix.1.gz \
         --slave /usr/share/man/man1/newaliases.1.gz mta-newaliasesman 
/usr/share/man/man1/newaliases.postfix.1.gz \
         --slave /usr/share/man/man8/sendmail.8.gz mta-sendmailman 
/usr/share/man/man1/sendmail.postfix.1.gz \
         --slave /usr/share/man/man5/aliases.5.gz mta-aliasesman 
/usr/share/man/man5/aliases.postfix.5.gz \
         --initscript postfix

  - Ian

-- 
ian at znark.com
http://www.znark.com/

From ehem at m5p.com  Wed Nov 17 00:26:45 2004
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Wed, 17 Nov 2004 00:26:45 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Hacking and Universities...
In-Reply-To: <20041117065620.02263593E@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200411170826.iAH8QjJL008750@m5p.com>

>From: Darkhorse <plug_0 at robinson-west.com>
> I realize a firewall isn't worth beans if it isn't up, the interesting
> thing even though there were rules to block outbound multicasts is that
> those rules didn't work.

> This iBook without some kind of firmware protection is too easily 
> hacked. Linux is the only system that appears to work correctly on 
> it if it can't be fixed.  We did a test with a ppc gentoo minimal
> disk and found it wasn't broadcasting those strange packets that it
> does when you're running MacOSX.  Schools use dangerous software like
> adobe acrobat, powerpoint, and MS-Word.  How are you supposed to look 
> at files from these programs if these files can not only virus, but in
> some cases completely destroy your computer?  If you can simulate a
> computer and protect the underlying hardware, that might not be so bad.

Why don't you provide more information about these "strange packets",
then we might be able to comment? Perhaps these are merely packets for
a legitimate application you're not familiar with.

Far more likely you're being hit by something known which has had a patch
available for weeks. You've said nothing about being up to date on
patches. No matter which OS you use, if you're behind on patches, you're
toast.


> The biggest problem I see is that protecting the firmware is an
> afterthought. It's useless to have a "good OS" if you don't protect
> the hardware foundations it runs on.  It's arrogant to say that the
> firmware in Apple iBooks doesn't get hacked, arrogant and stupid.

Well as I stated previously, yes the danger is there, but no so far no
one is known to of successfully written a virus which corrupts Apple
firmware. I note that x86 motherboards have also shipped with firmware
writable for quite some time, they're more common and they haven't been
hit yet.

This is certainly inviting disaster, but still, so far no one is known to
of even attempted it. Certainly writing to the firmware should be well
within the capabilities of many people out there (comment on most virus
writers, they're not very capable). Making it so that the machine remains
functional after modifying the firmware, and spreads the virus would be a
pretty massive undertaking. Note this sentence, once such a virus is
released, the most likely symptom is the machine becoming completely
unusable on reboot. It is highly unlikely that a machine hit by such a
virus would remain useful for too long.

There have not been ANY verified reports of virii which overwrite
firmware deliberatly for any system (expecting it to happen, but nothing
yet). It is highly unlikely your system was hit by something that hasn't
even been rumored to exist ANYWHERE. Heck with the prevalence of CD/DVD
writers autorun has been a wonderful target for a virus, yet there
haven't been any serious ones.

> Whoever thinks that MacOSX is more secure than Linux is full of it.

Yes, but that is a pretty high standard to compare to. A more useful
comparison would be "a little worse than Linux", "a lot worse than
Linux", "a little better than Windows", or "worse than Windows".

This just in from SANS:

  --Alliance Publishes ATM Cyber Security Guide
(10 November 2004)
The Global ATM Security Alliance has published a guide to cash machine
cyber security as a proactive measure; ATMs are increasingly moving to
Windows and other commonly used platforms.  Though ATMs are generally
on private networks or VPNs, the possibility of undocumented Internet
connections always exists, as does the possibility of malicious code
sneaking in via laptops connected to the system.  Last year, the Nachi
worm exploited the RPC DCOM vulnerability in Windows to compromise ATMs
at two different financial institutions.
http://www.securityfocus.com/printable/news/9903

I don't know about you, but the idea that my bank account(s) can be
reached from an ATM network based on Windows makes me woozy.

From keith at ahapala.net  Wed Nov 17 09:39:47 2004
From: keith at ahapala.net (Keith Nasman)
Date: Wed, 17 Nov 2004 09:39:47 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Securing tripwire/aide databases
Message-ID: <419B8CE3.4080602@ahapala.net>

I've used tripwire in the past and am using aide at the moment. Both of 
these programs recommend putting their databases on read-only media so 
that it can't be tampered with. I am currently putting my aide database 
on a floppy and leaving it mounted RO.

Two of my machines are remote and in doing updates to them I obviously 
create changes which aide dutifully reports. Since the db's are stored 
on the floppies I have to go on site to eject them, flip the tab, write 
the new db, flip the tab and remount RO. I'd like to find a remote 
solution where I can update the databases and still keep their integrity.

My first thought was to keep the databases on my server and then write a 
wrapper script to retrieve the db via ssh. It wasn't very hard for me to 
create a user on my server, exchange a pass phrase-less key and have the 
script retrieve the db. However, there isn't anything to prevent the 
uploading of a new db. I couldn't find that one can restrict an ssh key 
to be read only. I guess the ideal would be to have one key read 
only(without a passphrase) and another key that would require a typed 
password that would have read write ability on my server to upload the 
new db.

I suppose I could set up some sort of read-only ftp point on my server 
and then use ssh to upload the new db when needed.

So, any thoughts, gurus?

Thanks,
Keith


From russj at dimstar.net  Wed Nov 17 09:48:33 2004
From: russj at dimstar.net (Russ Johnson)
Date: Wed, 17 Nov 2004 09:48:33 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Securing tripwire/aide databases
In-Reply-To: <419B8CE3.4080602@ahapala.net>
References: <419B8CE3.4080602@ahapala.net>
Message-ID: <419B8EF1.7040903@dimstar.net>

Keith Nasman wrote:

> My first thought was to keep the databases on my server and then write 
> a wrapper script to retrieve the db via ssh.


In this situation, I think the best you can do is to keep a copy of the 
database off system. Then, if you suspect tampering, you can compare the 
off-site and on-site databases.

The other problem you might run into is modified binaries. How would you 
verify them? You would have to have a trusted system, somewhere to 
verify these things. So, if you trust your workstation, keeping the 
database there would be reasonable. Having your tripwire like substance 
check the binaries and periodically checking your databases would be 
reasonable.

Russ

integrity checker? Or do you mean other system binaries (ls, ps, top, 
etc)? If it is the latter then isn't that what the integrity checker is 
for? I've used the debian tool debsums and rpm --verify to check the 
binaries against the package installed versions. I also store a few 
important binaries on the floppies as well (ls, ps, netstat, etc).

I'm also going to look into samhain[1], which does both integrity 
checking and some level of intrusion detection as well. Apparently it 
can log to a central server which might be beneficial in my case.

Anyone have good/bad experiences with samhain?

Keith

1) http://la-samhna.de/samhain/index.html

I'm talking about the binaries that do the checking. /usr/sbin/tripwire 
or /usr/sbin/aide, etc.

Tripwire recommends that you keep copies of the database and binaries 
off system, on read only media, to check the system if compromise is 
suspected.

From clydefrog at adnap.no-ip.com  Wed Nov 17 18:29:22 2004
From: clydefrog at adnap.no-ip.com (Evan Heidtmann)
Date: Wed, 17 Nov 2004 18:29:22 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Solved!
In-Reply-To: <1100674430.7554.11.camel@timmy>
References: <1100674430.7554.11.camel@timmy>
Message-ID: <1100744962.6551.3.camel@timmy>

On Tue, 2004-11-16 at 22:53, Evan Heidtmann wrote:
> I have a bunch of graphs I need to put in my paper. They only need to be
> half a page wide, so to save space I would like to put them in a
> 2-column grid (side-by-side). 

I did some more searching and came up with this:

http://www.mail-archive.com/lyx-users at lists.lyx.org/msg33496.html

        My way of doing this is:
        Insert a figure float. Go inside, set the paragraph type to
        "satandard" instead of the default "caption" Insert two
        minipages inside the float
        (insert->minipage) with a hfill (insert->special->hfill) between
        them.
        Set each minipage to 50% of COL or less.
        In each minipage, include graphichs (insert->graphichs) Center
        the graphichs using layout->pragraph.
        Press enter after each graphich, to get a new paragraph. Set the
        paragraph type to caption, and enter caption text.
        
        Helge Hafting
        
Works for me!

There's a caption for each graphic (I put it above) and each appears as
a separate figure in the list of figures.

Evan

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Thu Nov 18 13:41:20 2004

References: <8A331D75F9F3CD4CA589854776E6F2E60364AEBC@hq-ex01.christenson.dom>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.60.0411181336160.9195@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Thu, 18 Nov 2004, Morthland, Cam wrote:

> Can anyone forsee why my spam filter keeps blocking this message?
>
> I added the sender to the whitelist, yet it is still blocked.

   Germane to the other reply posted on this thread, if you have postfix UCE
files (e.g., for IP addresses, domain names, header and body checks), put
that sender or domain at the top with and OK as the action.

   For example, in my /etc/postfix/badaddr file I have at the very top:

azzurri.jp                              OK 
moongroup.com                           OK 
eugenesand.com                          OK
greatbasin.net                          OK
nevadamining.org                        OK
gisnet.com                              OK

   They are passed through without hassle. I've never had any of these
tickled by spamassassin so I don't know if this is applicable to your
situation. Hope it helps, though.

Rich

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Thu Nov 18 22:52:43 2004
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (Darkhorse)
Date: 18 Nov 2004 22:52:43 -0800
Subject: ALMOST SOLVED  [PLUG] Hacking and Universities...
In-Reply-To: <200411170826.iAH8QjJL008750@m5p.com>
References: <200411170826.iAH8QjJL008750@m5p.com>
Message-ID: <1100847163.10984.20.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>

On Wed, 2004-11-17 at 00:26, Elliott Mitchell wrote:
> >From: Darkhorse <plug_0 at robinson-west.com>
> > I realize a firewall isn't worth beans if it isn't up, the interesting
> > thing even though there were rules to block outbound multicasts is that
> > those rules didn't work.
> 
> > This iBook without some kind of firmware protection is too easily 
> > hacked. Linux is the only system that appears to work correctly on 
> > it if it can't be fixed.  We did a test with a ppc gentoo minimal
> > disk and found it wasn't broadcasting those strange packets that it
> > does when you're running MacOSX.  Schools use dangerous software like
> > adobe acrobat, powerpoint, and MS-Word.  How are you supposed to look 
> > at files from these programs if these files can not only virus, but in
> > some cases completely destroy your computer?  If you can simulate a
> > computer and protect the underlying hardware, that might not be so bad.
> 
> Why don't you provide more information about these "strange packets",
> then we might be able to comment? Perhaps these are merely packets for
> a legitimate application you're not familiar with.

Anyone heard of mDNS or rendevous addresses?  That's what those
multicasts were all about.  Strangely when my brother bought an
identical ibook and compared it with his old one, the ttl on the
multicast packets were quite different.  On the old ibook the ttl 
is 255 whereas the ttl on the new iBook is just 1, the packet 
will stay in a local network.  He says he is learning about forth
and should be able to compare the firmware in both the new
machine and the old.

I thought apple was supposed to turn everything off by default following
the philosophy that what the user doesn't know about is dangerous to
that user.  I don't understand why these multicasts bypassed an ipfw
firewall set to all inbound and all outbound packets blocked.    
There are security problems that crop up when you don't need a server.  
This zeroconf networking idea is an interesting one, but I myself 
don't trust multicast dns yet and I consider this incident suggestive 
that Apple may be moving too fast.  

It's cool to see multicast for the first time.  Technically broadcast
addresses are a subset of multicast addresses, but that's different
than seeing an ip address of 224.0.0.253 when you're running tcpdump.

http://www.zeroconf.org
http://www.multicast.org

See informational sites at www.apple.com.  Multicast dns has been a
part of Mac OSX since version 10.2.

>

Tell windows to show you hidden and system files.

Samba knows that those are hidden files, so it sets the hidden bit when 
serving them to Windows.

In MY COMPUTER, go to TOOLS>FOLDER OPTIONS. Look on the VIEW tab.

Russ

Bill,

   I no longer run distributions named after hats, but I had a similar
experience -- twice -- when I upgraded from X.org-6.7.0 to -6.8.1 on the
Slackware-10.0 boxes here. When I reported it to freedesk as a bug they
responded that it's a known irritant but not a bug because it is in
/etc/X11/xorg.conf, and that's set up by the distributors, not freedesk.

   In my case the keyboard was not found. Turns out that in xorg.conf the
device is specified as "Keyboard" while the driver is named "keyboard".
Changed the uppercase 'K' to lowercase 'k' and the problem went away.

   On my fiancee's ThinkPad the default run level is GUI but I'm using kdm,
not gdm. Xfce works just fine with the former and it allows her to shutdown
without a root password or explicit sudo instructions from me.

From brian at brianquade.net  Sun Nov 21 09:45:01 2004
From: brian at brianquade.net (Brian Quade)
Date: Sun, 21 Nov 2004 09:45:01 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] ports not accessible to local network?
Message-ID: <41A0D41D.3030308@brianquade.net>

I have two machines on a network, rh9cups and fc2bind.
rh9cups runs RH9, has a printer attached, and runs CUPS as a server.
fc2bind runs Fedora Core2 and BIND as the authoritative server for the 
local domain.

rh9cups resolv.conf file lists fc2bind's IP address as its first name 
server, but running dig from rh9cups shows that it is using the 
secondary name server (the ISP) and is not resolving any of my local 
addresses.

 From fc2bind, if I run "nmap localhost" it shows 4 ports open (ssh, 
domain, ipp, rndc).
 From rh9cups, if I run "nmap 192.168.123.2" it shows only 1 port open 
(ssh).
(192.168.123.2 is the IP address of fc2bind)
Can a port be opened for local access only?  And if so, how can that be 
changed?
Is this an incorrectly configured BIND setup?

I am wondering if this is why the print server doesn't work either.  I 
had assumed it was just me because I have had trouble setting up CUPS in 
the past, but since rh9cups does not even see that fc2bind has an ipp 
port open I am wondering if it is the same problem.  fc2bind can see 
that rh9cups has an ipp port open, but it still does not see the printer.

Any suggestions?

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Sun Nov 21 17:31:46 2004
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Sun, 21 Nov 2004 17:31:46 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] ports not accessible to local network?
In-Reply-To: <41A0D41D.3030308@brianquade.net>
References: <41A0D41D.3030308@brianquade.net>
Message-ID: <1101087106.17986.8.camel@denk.nakedape.cc>

On Sun, 2004-11-21 at 09:45 -0800, Brian Quade wrote:
> I have two machines on a network, rh9cups and fc2bind.
> rh9cups runs RH9, has a printer attached, and runs CUPS as a server.
> fc2bind runs Fedora Core2 and BIND as the authoritative server for the 
> local domain.

> rh9cups resolv.conf file lists fc2bind's IP address as its first name 
> server, but running dig from rh9cups shows that it is using the 
> secondary name server (the ISP) and is not resolving any of my local 
> addresses.

This is normal; the resolver usually uses your DNS servers in round-
robin fashion.  You'd be fine on a network the size of yours just using
one.

>  From fc2bind, if I run "nmap localhost" it shows 4 ports open (ssh, 
> domain, ipp, rndc).
>  From rh9cups, if I run "nmap 192.168.123.2" it shows only 1 port open 
> (ssh).
> (192.168.123.2 is the IP address of fc2bind)
> Can a port be opened for local access only?  And if so, how can that be 
> changed?
> Is this an incorrectly configured BIND setup?

> I am wondering if this is why the print server doesn't work either.  I 
> had assumed it was just me because I have had trouble setting up CUPS in 
> the past, but since rh9cups does not even see that fc2bind has an ipp 
> port open I am wondering if it is the same problem.  fc2bind can see 
> that rh9cups has an ipp port open, but it still does not see the printer.

rh9cups shouldn't need to see the ipp port open on fc2bind, because
fc2bind connects as a client to the CUPS server.  It should, however,
see the 'domain' port open, although you scanned for TCP, and DNS
usually works through UDP.  However, it's probably the default firewall
setuo which is blocking both.  For the short term, run 'service iptables
stop' and see if you can get things working, then re-enabled the
firewall and configure it appropriately.

Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc>
Naked Ape Consulting, Ltd. <http://naked-ape.com>

Message-ID: <200411221443.58658.rsteff.l@comcast.net>

On Sunday 21 November 2004 3:19 pm, Kevin Schott wrote:

> I would like to just use a
> command-line interface to send immediate commands to the CP290. 

That's the direction in which I'd like to move, too. Have you worked with C? I 
have not, but I have code that is supposed to control a CP290. If we can 
extract the part that does that, get it to work, and ignore all the other 
things the program purports to do, we should be able to create a command line 
tool for the CP290. I also have the original programming guide for the CP290, 
so I think I have all the documentation needed to create all the options for 
the command line. 

Ideally, the command line program would accept parameters and would return 
whatever the CP290 spits out. Then, we could design other tools to make use 
of it, as we choose. Personally, I'd like to create a web interface so I can 
run it from around the house, and I'd like to set up the programming for it 
within a crontab. With a command line tool, both should be possible.

Regards,

Dick Steffens
http://home.comcast.net/~rsteff/

Message-ID: <20041124012741.GJ29577@bavariati.org>

On Sun, Nov 21, 2004 at 08:43:51AM -0800, Rich Shepard wrote:
> On Sat, 20 Nov 2004, Aaron Burt wrote:
> 
> >I'd guess that GPM is confused about how many buttons the trackball
> >has.  What happens if you paste using the right, middle or left+right
> >buttons?
> 
>   It used to mark with the left button and paste with the right button. I've
> tried right and both to no avail. In a xterm, shift-left copies to the mouse
> buffer and shift-both pastes from it.

Different from what I'm used to, but if it works for you, cool.

> >It would be helpful if you said what kind of trackball it is and what
> >mouse type GPM is configured for.  (Do a cat /etc/gpm.conf)
> 
>   No gpm.conf here. It's a Kensington Orbit two-button trackball and in
> /etc/rc.d/rc.gpm it's defined as
>   /usr/sbin/gpm -m /dev/mouse -t imps2
> 
> The regular, ps2 did not work.

Hrm.  Because the imps2 supports 3 buttons + scrollwheel, I'll bet
GPM thinks the right button on the trackball is the middle button.
As root, you should be able to try different GPM configs.  Just do an
/etc/rc.d/rc.gpm stop (does it work that way on your distro?)
and then try things like 'gpm -t autops2', try some cut'n'paste and
then do a 'gpm -k' to uninstall it.  the 'fups2' mouse type might work
for you.

Hey everybody-

I'm looking at going in with a few friends on a cheap 1U rackmountable
box to drop somewhere (preferably in the US) for a nice 'play' box.  It
will host a few OSS-related webpages, personal webpages, and other such
things.  I'm expecting to use no more than 50GB/month.

I'm looking for a good deal - a really good deal.  Most everybody offers
WAYYYYY more bandwidth than I want or need for more money than I want to
spend.

A few potential hits have been local - sterling.net and forked.net.
Have any of you guys had experience with either of them?  In your
opinions, are they reputable?  Do y'all know of others?

I'd ideally like somebody who can provide bandwidth when it's needed...
if I offer a 200K file, I don't want people to have to wait while it
downloads.  I'd also not like them to have to deal with high latencies.
I'd also rather not be on a bunch of blacklists because of neighbors.
But I don't want to pay for a bunch of bandwidth I never use.

Any thoughts?  :-)

On Wed, 24 Nov 2004, Rich Shepard wrote:

>    This seems to be debian-specific. Wnat to tar the binary and man page and
> ship the copy to me?

To get at the contents of a .deb package, first run "ar x somepackage.deb"
(s/x/xv/ for verbose mode) and then untar the file "data.tar.gz" (I'm not
sure it's called exactly that, but it's something like that) which was
extracted.

Sure.  I'm looking for 1U of space (I can build a 1U box for under $500)
and am only planning on using about 50GB/month worth of bandwidth, so
I'm trying to scale down lower than what most folks offer.

It looks like Kattare.com would offer it to me for about $200/mo.

Forked.net is offering me $50/mo, but asked about usage first, so I
think it's a special deal - I don't know the qualifications for the
deal.

Sterling.net hasn't gotten back to me with a price yet.  I responded to
their email asking me for more information on Tuesday night... hopefully
Friday or Monday I'll have something back from them.  They're my top
choice right now - I just hope they have a price that can compete with
Forked.

Inflow said they could work with me, but I haven't gotten around to
calling them yet.

Those are the local boys.

assertive.ca has a facility in Seattle and have a $100/mo special with a
lot of bandwidth.

Ziren sublets HE.net space - I think they have a facility in Seattle.
They offered $200/mo for 512Kbps in the 95th.

Those are all the companies who emailed me back when I asked them for
information.  There were probably another five I emailed on Monday.
ThePlanet.com, based in Texas, would do it for about $150/mo, as I
recall.  I saw several other webpages offering ~$150-200/mo with varying
amounts of bandwidth.  Kattare was the only one I can think of that
would cost me extra for the amount of bandwidth I'm using - they charge
a flat $4/GB.  To their credit, however, they offered and are now
hosting the primary US mirror for SlackSec and will continue to do so as
long as it stays under 1Mbps as measured at the 95th percentile.

From david.fleck at mchsi.com  Thu Nov 25 18:44:31 2004
From: david.fleck at mchsi.com (David Fleck)
Date: Thu, 25 Nov 2004 20:44:31 -0600 (CST)
Subject: [PLUG] FreeBSD 5.3 installation problems
In-Reply-To: <20041125200754.17028.qmail@web50608.mail.yahoo.com>
References: <20041125200754.17028.qmail@web50608.mail.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <20041125203028.J48346@grond.sourballs.org>

On Thu, 25 Nov 2004, Robert Kopp wrote:
> This is primarily a Linux list, but I thought I'd
> experiment with FreeBSD, and after installing it (X
> working) I find a few residual problems:

First off, some very helpful mailing lists for FreeBSD are 
freebsd-questions at freebsd.org and freebsd-stable at freebsd.org.
(http://www.freebsd.org/doc/en_US.ISO8859-1/books/handbook/eresources.html#ERESOURCES-SUMMARY)

I can't provide much specific help, as I'm on an older version (4.9) and 
using an older Radeon card (7500).  The 'stable' mailing list can be 
especially helpful with hardware issues.

On the sound card issue - do you know what the correct driver for your 
card is?  I think it might be the 'pcm' driver, but I'm not sure.  Is the 
driver loaded?  If so, the kernel should make some noise when it finds the 
card, e.g.:

  pcm0: <CMedia CMI8738> port 0xb800-0xb8ff irq 10 at device 5.0 on pci0
  pcm0: sndbuf_setmap 1000, 4000; 0xf26f9000 -> 1000
  pcm0: sndbuf_setmap 5000, 4000; 0xf26fd000 -> 5000

check dmesg output.  Also, have you read the sound section of the 
Handbook? 
(http://www.freebsd.org/doc/en_US.ISO8859-1/books/handbook/sound-setup.html)
it should help you figure out in general what's going on.


--
David Fleck
david.fleck at mchsi.com

Message-ID: <1101533270.11994.15.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>

I'm trying to get samba 2.2.8a to work with Windows 2000 where 
I'm running into something called Active directory.  Apparently 
it requires a strange DNS setup where I use Bind 8.4 from ISC, 
not Windows 2000 Server.  

I want to get 2000 networked with non Windows 2000 computers.  
Can active directory be completely disabled or am I stuck with
it in 2000 professional?

I can use some dns and samba tips for Windows 2000 professional.

The only other alternative I can think of is Windows 2000 on
top of Linux and don't network it.  That would be a pain
though, printing etc. would be difficult.  I don't even know
if Windows 2000 can run in vmware.  As far as Mapinfo, 
MyFamily.com, TurboTax, etc. I'm wondering if Windows is
even needed anymore?

Windows 2000 pro locks up on my network and has trouble finding
other computers.  I've tried to set up a samba 2.2.8a primary
domain controller, but I keep getting dns errors when I attempt
to switch the network identification from workgroup to domain.  
My smb.conf is attached for the samba based PDC I tried to set 
up.  My domain is called domain01 and I did a dns A record
for this.  Microsoft's site says something about SRV records.
Does Active Directory mean that you have to do dynamic dns
even if you don't want to for security reasons?

     Michael C. Robinson

# Samba config file created using SWAT
# from localhost (127.0.0.1)
# Date: 2004/11/25 12:50:58

# Global parameters
[global]
	netbios name = GOOSE2
	workgroup = DOMAIN01
	os level = 64
	preferred master = True
	domain master = True
        local master = True
        security = user
	encrypt passwords = Yes
	domain logons = Yes

	interfaces = eth0:1
	bind interfaces only = Yes

	log file = /var/log/samba/log.%m
	log level = 1
	socket options = TCP_NODELAY SO_RCVBUF=8192 SO_SNDBUF=8192
	logon path = \\%N\home\%u\windows
	logon drive = H:
	logon home = \\homeserver\%u\windows
	logon script = logon.cmd

	hosts allow = 192.168.2.,127.0.0.1
	hosts deny = ALL
        wins support= Yes

[netlogon]
	path = /usr/lib/samba/netlogon
	write list = ntadmin

[profiles]
	path = /usr/lib/samba/ntprofile
	read only = No
	create mask = 0600
	directory mask = 0700

[tmp]
	path = /tmp
        read only = No
        public = Yes

[home]
	comment = Home Directories
	path = /home
	read only = No
        public = No

[printers]
	comment = All Printers
	path = /var/spool/samba
	printable = Yes
	browseable = No

[net-2]
	comment = netbooted share
	path = /opt/net-2

From redhat at crisponions.com  Sat Nov 27 10:30:29 2004
From: redhat at crisponions.com (Len Icks)
Date: Sat, 27 Nov 2004 10:30:29 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] SAMBA and windows 2000...
Message-ID: <FEB04E5E003F56499D8B8B1AF4409646577C@jinx.crisponions.com>

Technically you don't need active directory on a 2000 pro workstation as
long as you have it a member of a workgroup, when you make 2000 a member
of a domain it uses active directory, which requires a 200X domain
controller.

I cant really give you any tips on security for this set up as I usually
keep my samba pretty simple.  I have my samba server as a member of a
workgroup (which should be the same name as the workgroup on your 2000
PC) and have my security level set to user.  This lets me share folders
and printers between my 2 different OS's with minimal difficulty.

Another alternative is to move to samba 3.X, which has more features for
integrating AD and samba together in a domain type environment.

Sounds to me though like you are looking to just do some home network
sharing.

My home network is also a workgroup (XP Pro and Linux (which is a dual boot
between Linux and XP Pro)). I do not use Active Directory. In fact you need
a Microsoft server like Server 2003 or the older 2K Server to run Active
Directory and manage a domain.

I puzzled sharing files with Linux a while back and wrote up what I found
(samba, active directory, primary domain controllers, LDAP, WINS, etc.). I
understand I cannot attach a doc to this mail. If you want to reply off this
mailing list to tkubaska at ieee.org, I'll send the file to you. Cannot
guarrentee it will help but Windows and Linux can work well together...well,
depends what you want to do, but they share files just fine.

Has PLUG ever had a talk on Windows/Linux interoperability?

 -Ted

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Sat Nov 27 13:44:03 2004
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Sat, 27 Nov 2004 13:44:03 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] SAMBA and windows 2000...
In-Reply-To: <001c01c4d4c7$64abd620$6401a8c0@beachparty>
References: <001c01c4d4c7$64abd620$6401a8c0@beachparty>
Message-ID: <1101591843.6696.12.camel@denk.nakedape.cc>

On Sat, 2004-11-27 at 13:23 -0800, Ted Kubaska wrote:

> Has PLUG ever had a talk on Windows/Linux interoperability?

Occasionally through out the years.  I did an AT talk a few months back
about using Samba 3 as domain controllers and using LDAP as a replicated
authentication database.  Using Samba can get pretty involved,
considering that Microsoft has changed or added new methods with every
release, and Samba has nobs that let you operate with or without most of
these.  That said, a basic setup for the "average home network" (if
there is such a thing) is pretty straight-forward and hasn't changed
much over the years.

Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc>
Naked Ape Consulting, Ltd. <http://naked-ape.com>

It's possible to uninstall Microsoft Networking, what's to stop someone
from starting an OSS project to develop a file sharing program
installable to any version of Windows or Linux?  Samba's approach is to
attempt to have a program that can simulate Microsoft's server, why not 
replace the client in Windows?  The hooks into various programs wouldn't
line up, but for starters it would be nice to simply have something
that's more convenient than ftp and encrypted.

I am toying with the idea of using Novell Netware.  Can Netware
integrate with old versions of Windows such as 98SE and 2000?  
I don't know the cost of Netware.  I have a student copy of 
Netware 4.7 that I got at the time when PCC was just switching 
over to Netware 5.  My 98 clients tie into a samba based domain,
I think that's why when I put the 2000 box in workgroup mode
that it isn't visible on the 98 boxes and vice versa.  I like
the mounting scripts, but time synchronization would be better
done via ntp.  With profiles, I don't think they are of any use
unless all of your Windows workstations have exactly the same
hardware and software configuration.  You'll load a profile 
from one computer onto  another and find that there are tons 
of broken links.  I am debating the value of a wins server too, 
I can't get 2000 pro workstation to use the one I've set up even 
though 98SE and even XP seem to be able to use it just fine.  
Using a local lmhosts file, even though this is harder to maintain, 
can cut down on network congestion.  If Novell would open source
it's networking technologies and make it's money training and
providing custom solutions, I'll bet that it would just soar.

I'm curious if I abandon doing a domain and put all my Winduhs boxes
in workstation mode if I'll be able to see the 2000 box and vice
versa.

Samba is an impressive piece of software, I'd rather see the client
on the Microsoft end replaced with something better though so that
your filesharing server can be coded better.

From heinlein at madboa.com  Sun Nov 28 05:25:34 2004
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Sun, 28 Nov 2004 05:25:34 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] any gcc/gtk+ people out there?
In-Reply-To: <1101625596.18228.2.camel@denk.nakedape.cc>
References: <41A8FF53.8090108@brianquade.net> <86zn12x3ph.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
	<1101625596.18228.2.camel@denk.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0411280514440.2943@pecos.madboa.com>

On Sat, 27 Nov 2004, Wil Cooley wrote:

>> Brian> /tmp/ccOTj6pS.o(.eh_frame+0x11): undefined reference to
>> Brian> `__gxx_personality_v0' collect2: ld returned 1 exit status
> ...
>> I don't know for sure how this translates to RH, but in debian-land 
>> there are a bunch of -dev packages with headers and such that are 
>> not needed to just run binaries, but are needed to compile things 
>> that use the libraries.  I'd advise looking for those.
>
> He'd have gotten a compiler error trying to include <gtk/gtk.h>, but 
> he's getting a linker error.  He probably needs to include several 
> libraries with '-l'.  Brian, tell us how you're running gcc exactly.

The __gxx_personality_v0 stuff is part of the g++ exception-handling 
routines. Make sure the libstdc++-devel rpm is installed.

When dealing with gtk, perhaps the easiest way to set your conpiler 
flags is to use gtk-config (part of the gtk+-devel rpm):

   gcc \
     `gtk-config --cflags gtk` \
     `gtk-config --libs gtk` \
     -o foo \
     foo.c`

-- Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com>


From griffint at pobox.com  Sun Nov 28 15:57:04 2004
From: griffint at pobox.com (Terry Griffin)
Date: Sun, 28 Nov 2004 15:57:04 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] New X10 controller software available (was: Serial Port
	Programming)
In-Reply-To: <200411222102.41619.griffint@pobox.com>
References: <009b01c4d020$95010760$0300a8c0@bass>
	<200411222102.41619.griffint@pobox.com>
Message-ID: <200411281557.04989.griffint@pobox.com>

It's time to expose my software to a wider world. Find the first
release of the 'x10py' package here:

 http://www.blindchicken.com/~terryg/x10py/

I'd previously expressed concern that I would not be able to get
sufficiently accurate serial I/O timing with Python but this has
not been a problem. Without any changes to the timing delays this
software has been successfully tested on machines from a 133MHz
Pentium to a 2.8GHz P4.

As I warned before there are no applications or utilities yet. But
there's lots of sample code provided, so with just minimal knowledge
of Python you should be able to kludge up something useful.

Enjoy,

Terry Griffin

Just use your oscilloscope. Or...

What I usually do is plug in an old serial mouse and fire up the gpm
program with the serial port options. (See the gpm man page.) If I
can move the console mouse cursor around using the serial mouse then
I know the port works.

      **********************************************************
          
                                 PRESENTATION

                               An Introduction
                                      to
                                  Subversion

                                      by

                                 Frank Torres
                           <frank.t326 at Comcast.net>


      Programmers (and other creative artists) need version control
      systems to coordinate different contributors' work and to keep 
      track of all the different releases or versions of a project.  
      In recent years, many people in the Open Source community have 
      used Concurrent Version System (cvs) for this.  cvs works well,
      but it has a number of problems and limitations.  

      Subversion (http://subversion.tigris.org/) is a replacement
      to cvs which has most of cvs's features without some of the
      problems and limitations of cvs.
      
      **********************************************************

From heinlein at madboa.com  Mon Nov 29 09:20:17 2004
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Mon, 29 Nov 2004 09:20:17 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Firefox and PDF -- again lost path to xpdf
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.60.0411290817180.9162@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.60.0411290817180.9162@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0411290907490.3057@glenfiddich.cse.ogi.edu>

On Mon, 29 Nov 2004, Rich Shepard wrote:

>  I upgraded to firefox-1.0. Now when I want to open a pdf file to 
> view using xpdf I need to manually set the path each time. In Edit 
> -> Preferences -> Downloads there's nothing in the box and no 
> enabled button that would allow me to associate /usr/local/bin/xpdf 
> with pdf files. When I open about:config I find nothing under 'pdf', 
> 'download' or 'open'. Can't find anything that looks appropriate.
>
>  If anyone knows how to set this up, please pass me a pointer to the 
> technique.

Firefox seems to default to your mailcap settings (~/.mailcap, 
/etc/mailcap), so you can set things there, if you'd like:

   echo "application/pdf; xpdf %s" >> ~/.mailcap

Otherwise, once you get to the "What should Firefox do with this 
file?" dialog box, enter xpdf and then click the "Do this 
automatically for files like this from now on" button.

At that point, you can edit those settings in the Preferences -> 
Downloads dialog box.

If you're up for some serious spelunking, check out the mimeTypes.rdf 
file buried in your ~/.mozilla/firefox directory tree.

--Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com>

From jason at vancleve.com  Mon Nov 29 10:43:59 2004

Subject: [PLUG] Getting Moz' To Use XPDF

From rsteff.l at comcast.net  Mon Nov 29 14:23:44 2004
From: rsteff.l at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)
Date: Mon, 29 Nov 2004 14:23:44 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Serial Port Programming
In-Reply-To: <BAY20-F5A4CB0CC9115455E3E11DB8BD0@phx.gbl>
References: <BAY20-F5A4CB0CC9115455E3E11DB8BD0@phx.gbl>
Message-ID: <200411291423.44732.rsteff.l@comcast.net>

On Monday 29 November 2004 2:01 pm, Vincent Yau wrote:

> I did not know that serial port turn off by default.  I am on a desktop,
> does that rule still apply?

Maybe.

The easiest way to know is to re-boot and hit whatever your machine uses to 
look at and modify your BIOS settings. On one of my machines that's the DEL 
key, on another it's the F1 or F2 key (I think). But, you should see some 
wording to that effect on the screen. Once you're looking at the BIOS, you 
will have to do a bit of digging around, since different BIOS's handle things 
differently. It may be in a section labeled Advanced. What you're looking for 
is something related to ports. Once you find that, look for serial or comm 
ports and make sure that the one you're using is not disabled.  This should 
make a lot more sense when you see it. 

Hope this helps.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens
http://home.comcast.net/~rsteff/


From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Mon Nov 29 14:30:03 2004
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Mon, 29 Nov 2004 14:30:03 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] What??
In-Reply-To: <1101718667.4474.228.camel@localhost.localdomain>
References: <1101718667.4474.228.camel@localhost.localdomain>
Message-ID: <1101767403.17504.33.camel@denk.nakedape.cc>

On Mon, 2004-11-29 at 00:57 -0800, Vram wrote:
> What is this all about??
> 
> 
> System Events
> =-=-=-=-=-=-=
> Nov 28 22:32:18 kosmal postfix/smtp[4011]: connect to
> chinabytemail.com[64.40.102.42]: Connection refused
> (port 25)
...
> Nov 28 22:48:58 kosmal named[240]: unrelated additional info
> 'dns1.name-services.com' type A from
> [63.251.163.102].53
> Nov 28 22:50:58 kosmal named[240]: NSTATS 1101711058 1095662893 A=5647
> NS=3 CNAME=87 SOA=17 PTR=3860 MX=3256
> TXT=10 AAAA=755 A6=114 ANY=2
>
> Are they trying to add an A record????

Yes. it's called DNS cache poisoning.  BIND hasn't been typically
vulernable for a long time.  However, you have to be careful with nscd
too--I found some things weren't working correctly because 'localhost'
was giving me an address other than 127.0.0.1.  BIND hasn't cached the
error, but it propagated it to nscd's cache.  For that reason, I
recommend editing /etc/nscd.conf and using:

        enable-cache            hosts           no

It's okay to cache group and password data, since that always comes from
trusted sources.  There's not much point in caching hosts data, since
your DNS server will do that for DNS records and /etc/hosts access is
quite fast and few people use LDAP or other NSS sources for hosts data.

BTW, I discovered this using the 'getent' command, which gives you a
libc-view of your NSS data.  (Of course, some applications won't use the
libc functions for hosts data and will implement their own resolver or
use the DNS resolver directly.)

For the curioius, there's some information about NSS here:

http://nakedape.cc/wiki/ApplicationNotes/NameServiceSwitch

I should actually add this info...

Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc>
Naked Ape Consulting, Ltd. <http://naked-ape.com>

You don't have to keep them, but you might want to (the tarballs, anyway).

You might wish to re-install someday, or look at (or even change!) the code,
or maybe even remove the software.  (If the packagers were nice, then 
'make uninstall' will do this.)

I wouldn't leave the tarballs in a tmp directory, though.

From rich at paranoid.org  Tue Nov 30 12:42:04 2004
From: rich at paranoid.org (Rich Burroughs)
Date: Tue, 30 Nov 2004 12:42:04 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] SSH question
In-Reply-To: <41b0d424.6858130@mail.aracnet.com>
References: <41ac2b73.13858335@mail.aracnet.com>
	<41b0d424.6858130@mail.aracnet.com>
Message-ID: <41ACDB1C.8030003@paranoid.org>

techmage at aracnet.com wrote:
> When I try to shh from Suse 7 back to itself I get an error:
> 
> ssh 192.*.*.* port 22  : connection refused
> 
> I can even telnet from suse 7 back into itself, just not ssh
> 
> The info from the Novell site was interesting, but did not address the
> issue, which I think may be tied to tcp-wrappers info except that I
> can telnet but not ssh

It depends on how your sshd is configured to run, whether it's standlone 
or run through the TCP wrappers. I haven't used SUSE much so I'm not 
sure how they set it up, but a lot of people tend to run it standalone 
since there's some overhead when is starts up.

If it is being run through the TCP wrappers, then you'll see an entry 
for it in the TCP wrappers conf file.

"Connection refused" is an error that rules out things like problems 
with keys. You're not making a connection at all. If you're getting that 
when you try to SSH back into itself, that certainly rules out network 
problems.

A common cause for those "connection refused" errors is when you try to 
connect to a port where no service is running. The most likely problem 
is that sshd is just not listening there. Perhaps it was not installed 
when you rebuilt the box, or it is not configured to start up properly. 
That's the first thing I'd check. If it's not in the process list, see 
if it's installed, and if you can start it up by hand.

Beyond that, check it's access restrictions, and any firewalling rules 
you have set up.

As for the fact that telnet works, that doesn't mean much since they are 
different services on different ports. It may be pertinent if telnet and 
sshd are both run through the TCP wrappers and their config is the same, 
though.


Rich
